[52] CHAPTER I.[53] I GO TO STYLES[54] [13]
[55] [13]
[56] The intense interest aroused in the public by what was known at the[57] time as “The Styles Case” has now somewhat subsided. Nevertheless, in[58] view of the world-wide notoriety which attended it, I have been asked,[59] both by my friend Poirot and the family themselves, to write an account[60] of the whole story. This, we trust, will effectually silence the[61] sensational rumours which still persist.[62] [13]
[63] I will therefore briefly set down the circumstances which led to my[64] being connected with the affair.[65] [13]
[66] I had been invalided home from the Front; and, after spending some[67] months in a rather depressing Convalescent Home, was given a month’s[68] sick leave. Having no near relations or friends, I was trying to make[69] up my mind what to do, when I ran across John Cavendish. I had seen[70] very little of him for some years. Indeed, I had never known him[71] particularly well. He was a good fifteen years my senior, for one[72] thing, though he hardly looked his forty-five years. As a boy, though,[73] I had often stayed at Styles, his mother’s place in Essex.[74] [13]
[75] We had a good yarn about old times, and it ended in his inviting me[76] down to Styles to spend my leave there.[77] [13]
[78] “The mater will be delighted to see you again—after all those years,”[79] he added.[80] [13]
[81] “Your mother keeps well?” I asked.[82] [13]
[83] “Oh, yes. I suppose you know that she has married again?”[84] [13]
[85] I am afraid I showed my surprise rather plainly. Mrs. Cavendish, who[86] had married John’s father when he was a widower with two sons, had been[87] a handsome woman of middle-age as I remembered her. She certainly could[88] not be a day less than seventy now. I recalled her as an energetic,[89] autocratic personality, somewhat inclined to charitable and social[90] notoriety, with a fondness for opening bazaars and playing the Lady[91] Bountiful. She was a most generous woman, and possessed a considerable[92] fortune of her own.[93] [13]
[94] Their country-place, Styles Court, had been purchased by Mr. Cavendish[95] early in their married life. He had been completely under his wife’s[96] ascendancy, so much so that, on dying, he left the place to her for her[97] lifetime, as well as the larger part of his income; an arrangement that[98] was distinctly unfair to his two sons. Their step-mother, however, had[99] always been most generous to them; indeed, they were so young at the[100] time of their father’s remarriage that they always thought of her as[101] their own mother.[102] [13]
[103] Lawrence, the younger, had been a delicate youth. He had qualified as a[104] doctor but early relinquished the profession of medicine, and lived at[105] home while pursuing literary ambitions; though his verses never had any[106] marked success.[107] [13]
[108] John practised for some time as a barrister, but had finally settled[109] down to the more congenial life of a country squire. He had married two[110] years ago, and had taken his wife to live at Styles, though I[111] entertained a shrewd suspicion that he would have preferred his mother[112] to increase his allowance, which would have enabled him to have a home[113] of his own. Mrs. Cavendish, however, was a lady who liked to make her[114] own plans, and expected other people to fall in with them, and in this[115] case she certainly had the whip hand, namely: the purse strings.[116] [13]
[117] John noticed my surprise at the news of his mother’s remarriage and[118] smiled rather ruefully.[119] [13]
[120] “Rotten little bounder too!” he said savagely. “I can tell you,[121] Hastings, it’s making life jolly difficult for us. As for Evie—you[122] remember Evie?”[123] [13]
[124] “No.”[125] [13]
[126] “Oh, I suppose she was after your time. She’s the mater’s factotum,[127] companion, Jack of all trades! A great sport—old Evie! Not precisely[128] young and beautiful, but as game as they make them.”[129] [13]
[130] “You were going to say——?”[131] [13]
[132] “Oh, this fellow! He turned up from nowhere, on the pretext of being a[133] second cousin or something of Evie’s, though she didn’t seem[134] particularly keen to acknowledge the relationship. The fellow is an[135] absolute outsider, anyone can see that. He’s got a great black beard,[136] and wears patent leather boots in all weathers! But the mater cottoned[137] to him at once, took him on as secretary—you know how she’s always[138] running a hundred societies?”[139] [13]
[140] I nodded.[141] [13]
[142] “Well, of course the war has turned the hundreds into thousands. No[143] doubt the fellow was very useful to her. But you could have knocked us[144] all down with a feather when, three months ago, she suddenly announced[145] that she and Alfred were engaged! The fellow must be at least twenty[146] years younger than she is! It’s simply bare-faced fortune hunting; but[147] there you are—she is her own mistress, and she’s married him.”[148] [13]
[149] “It must be a difficult situation for you all.”[150] [13]
[151] “Difficult! It’s damnable!”[152] [13]
[153] Thus it came about that, three days later, I descended from the train[154] at Styles St. Mary, an absurd little station, with no apparent reason[155] for existence, perched up in the midst of green fields and country[156] lanes. John Cavendish was waiting on the platform, and piloted me out[157] to the car.[158] [13]
[159] “Got a drop or two of petrol still, you see,” he remarked. “Mainly[160] owing to the mater’s activities.”[161] [13]
[162] The village of Styles St. Mary was situated about two miles from the[163] little station, and Styles Court lay a mile the other side of it. It[164] was a still, warm day in early July. As one looked out over the flat[165] Essex country, lying so green and peaceful under the afternoon sun, it[166] seemed almost impossible to believe that, not so very far away, a great[167] war was running its appointed course. I felt I had suddenly strayed[168] into another world. As we turned in at the lodge gates, John said:[169] [13]
[170] “I’m afraid you’ll find it very quiet down here, Hastings.”[171] [13]
[172] “My dear fellow, that’s just what I want.”[173] [13]
[174] “Oh, it’s pleasant enough if you want to lead the idle life. I drill[175] with the volunteers twice a week, and lend a hand at the farms. My wife[176] works regularly ‘on the land’. She is up at five every morning to milk,[177] and keeps at it steadily until lunchtime. It’s a jolly good life taking[178] it all round—if it weren’t for that fellow Alfred Inglethorp!” He[179] checked the car suddenly, and glanced at his watch. “I wonder if we’ve[180] time to pick up Cynthia. No, she’ll have started from the hospital by[181] now.”[182] [13]
[183] “Cynthia! That’s not your wife?”[184] [13]
[185] “No, Cynthia is a protégée of my mother’s, the daughter of an old[186] schoolfellow of hers, who married a rascally solicitor. He came a[187] cropper, and the girl was left an orphan and penniless. My mother came[188] to the rescue, and Cynthia has been with us nearly two years now. She[189] works in the Red Cross Hospital at Tadminster, seven miles away.”[190] [13]
[191] As he spoke the last words, we drew up in front of the fine old house.[192] A lady in a stout tweed skirt, who was bending over a flower bed,[193] straightened herself at our approach.[194] [13]
[195] “Hullo, Evie, here’s our wounded hero! Mr. Hastings—Miss Howard.”[196] [13]
[197] Miss Howard shook hands with a hearty, almost painful, grip. I had an[198] impression of very blue eyes in a sunburnt face. She was a[199] pleasant-looking woman of about forty, with a deep voice, almost manly[200] in its stentorian tones, and had a large sensible square body, with[201] feet to match—these last encased in good thick boots. Her conversation,[202] I soon found, was couched in the telegraphic style.[203] [13]
[204] “Weeds grow like house afire. Can’t keep even with ’em. Shall press you[205] in. Better be careful.”[206] [13]
[207] “I’m sure I shall be only too delighted to make myself useful,” I[208] responded.[209] [13]
[210] “Don’t say it. Never does. Wish you hadn’t later.”[211] [13]
[212] “You’re a cynic, Evie,” said John, laughing. “Where’s tea to-day—inside[213] or out?”[214] [13]
[215] “Out. Too fine a day to be cooped up in the house.”[216] [13]
[217] “Come on then, you’ve done enough gardening for to-day. ‘The labourer[218] is worthy of his hire’, you know. Come and be refreshed.”[219] [13]
[220] “Well,” said Miss Howard, drawing off her gardening gloves, “I’m[221] inclined to agree with you.”[222] [13]
[223] She led the way round the house to where tea was spread under the shade[224] of a large sycamore.[225] [13]
[226] A figure rose from one of the basket chairs, and came a few steps to[227] meet us.[228] [13]
[229] “My wife, Hastings,” said John.[230] [13]
[231] I shall never forget my first sight of Mary Cavendish. Her tall,[232] slender form, outlined against the bright light; the vivid sense of[233] slumbering fire that seemed to find expression only in those wonderful[234] tawny eyes of hers, remarkable eyes, different from any other woman’s[235] that I have ever known; the intense power of stillness she possessed,[236] which nevertheless conveyed the impression of a wild untamed spirit in[237] an exquisitely civilised body—all these things are burnt into my[238] memory. I shall never forget them.[239] [13]
[240] She greeted me with a few words of pleasant welcome in a low clear[241] voice, and I sank into a basket chair feeling distinctly glad that I[242] had accepted John’s invitation. Mrs. Cavendish gave me some tea, and[243] her few quiet remarks heightened my first impression of her as a[244] thoroughly fascinating woman. An appreciative listener is always[245] stimulating, and I described, in a humorous manner, certain incidents[246] of my Convalescent Home, in a way which, I flatter myself, greatly[247] amused my hostess. John, of course, good fellow though he is, could[248] hardly be called a brilliant conversationalist.[249] [13]
[250] At that moment a well remembered voice floated through the open French[251] window near at hand:[252] [13]
[253] “Then you’ll write to the Princess after tea, Alfred? I’ll write to[254] Lady Tadminster for the second day, myself. Or shall we wait until we[255] hear from the Princess? In case of a refusal, Lady Tadminster might[256] open it the first day, and Mrs. Crosbie the second. Then there’s the[257] Duchess—about the school fête.”[258] [13]
[259] There was the murmur of a man’s voice, and then Mrs. Inglethorp’s rose[260] in reply:[261] [13]
[262] “Yes, certainly. After tea will do quite well. You are so thoughtful,[263] Alfred dear.”[264] [13]
[265] The French window swung open a little wider, and a handsome[266] white-haired old lady, with a somewhat masterful cast of features,[267] stepped out of it on to the lawn. A man followed her, a suggestion of[268] deference in his manner.[269] [13]
[270] Mrs. Inglethorp greeted me with effusion.[271] [13]
[272] “Why, if it isn’t too delightful to see you again, Mr. Hastings, after[273] all these years. Alfred, darling, Mr. Hastings—my husband.”[274] [13]
[275] I looked with some curiosity at “Alfred darling”. He certainly struck a[276] rather alien note. I did not wonder at John objecting to his beard. It[277] was one of the longest and blackest I have ever seen. He wore[278] gold-rimmed pince-nez, and had a curious impassivity of feature. It[279] struck me that he might look natural on a stage, but was strangely out[280] of place in real life. His voice was rather deep and unctuous. He[281] placed a wooden hand in mine and said:[282] [13]
[283] “This is a pleasure, Mr. Hastings.” Then, turning to his wife: “Emily[284] dearest, I think that cushion is a little damp.”[285] [13]
[286] She beamed fondly on him, as he substituted another with every[287] demonstration of the tenderest care. Strange infatuation of an[288] otherwise sensible woman![289] [13]
[290] With the presence of Mr. Inglethorp, a sense of constraint and veiled[291] hostility seemed to settle down upon the company. Miss Howard, in[292] particular, took no pains to conceal her feelings. Mrs. Inglethorp,[293] however, seemed to notice nothing unusual. Her volubility, which I[294] remembered of old, had lost nothing in the intervening years, and she[295] poured out a steady flood of conversation, mainly on the subject of the[296] forthcoming bazaar which she was organizing and which was to take place[297] shortly. Occasionally she referred to her husband over a question of[298] days or dates. His watchful and attentive manner never varied. From the[299] very first I took a firm and rooted dislike to him, and I flatter[300] myself that my first judgments are usually fairly shrewd.[301] [13]
[302] Presently Mrs. Inglethorp turned to give some instructions about[303] letters to Evelyn Howard, and her husband addressed me in his[304] painstaking voice:[305] [13]
[306] “Is soldiering your regular profession, Mr. Hastings?”[307] [13]
[308] “No, before the war I was in Lloyd’s.”[309] [13]
[310] “And you will return there after it is over?”[311] [13]
[312] “Perhaps. Either that or a fresh start altogether.”[313] [13]
[314] Mary Cavendish leant forward.[315] [13]
[316] “What would you really choose as a profession, if you could just[317] consult your inclination?”[318] [13]
[319] “Well, that depends.”[320] [13]
[321] “No secret hobby?” she asked. “Tell me—you’re drawn to something?[322] Everyone is—usually something absurd.”[323] [13]
[324] “You’ll laugh at me.”[325] [13]
[326] She smiled.[327] [13]
[328] “Perhaps.”[329] [13]
[330] “Well, I’ve always had a secret hankering to be a detective!”[331] [13]
[332] “The real thing—Scotland Yard? Or Sherlock Holmes?”[333] [13]
[334] “Oh, Sherlock Holmes by all means. But really, seriously, I am awfully[335] drawn to it. I came across a man in Belgium once, a very famous[336] detective, and he quite inflamed me. He was a marvellous little fellow.[337] He used to say that all good detective work was a mere matter of[338] method. My system is based on his—though of course I have progressed[339] rather further. He was a funny little man, a great dandy, but[340] wonderfully clever.”[341] [13]
[342] “Like a good detective story myself,” remarked Miss Howard. “Lots of[343] nonsense written, though. Criminal discovered in last chapter. Everyone[344] dumbfounded. Real crime—you’d know at once.”[345] [13]
[346] “There have been a great number of undiscovered crimes,” I argued.[347] [13]
[348] “Don’t mean the police, but the people that are right in it. The[349] family. You couldn’t really hoodwink them. They’d know.”[350] [13]
[351] “Then,” I said, much amused, “you think that if you were mixed up in a[352] crime, say a murder, you’d be able to spot the murderer right off?”[353] [13]
[354] “Of course I should. Mightn’t be able to prove it to a pack of lawyers.[355] But I’m certain I’d know. I’d feel it in my fingertips if he came near[356] me.”[357] [13]
[358] “It might be a ‘she’,” I suggested.[359] [13]
[360] “Might. But murder’s a violent crime. Associate it more with a man.”[361] [13]
[362] “Not in a case of poisoning.” Mrs. Cavendish’s clear voice startled me.[363] “Dr. Bauerstein was saying yesterday that, owing to the general[364] ignorance of the more uncommon poisons among the medical profession,[365] there were probably countless cases of poisoning quite unsuspected.”[366] [13]
[367] “Why, Mary, what a gruesome conversation!” cried Mrs. Inglethorp. “It[368] makes me feel as if a goose were walking over my grave. Oh, there’s[369] Cynthia!”[370] [13]
[371] A young girl in V.A.D. uniform ran lightly across the lawn.[372] [13]
[373] “Why, Cynthia, you are late to-day. This is Mr. Hastings—Miss Murdoch.”[374] [13]
[375] Cynthia Murdoch was a fresh-looking young creature, full of life and[376] vigour. She tossed off her little V.A.D. cap, and I admired the great[377] loose waves of her auburn hair, and the smallness and whiteness of the[378] hand she held out to claim her tea. With dark eyes and eyelashes she[379] would have been a beauty.[380] [13]
[381] She flung herself down on the ground beside John, and as I handed her a[382] plate of sandwiches she smiled up at me.[383] [13]
[384] “Sit down here on the grass, do. It’s ever so much nicer.”[385] [13]
[386] I dropped down obediently.[387] [13]
[388] “You work at Tadminster, don’t you, Miss Murdoch?”[389] [13]
[390] She nodded.[391] [13]
[392] “For my sins.”[393] [13]
[394] “Do they bully you, then?” I asked, smiling.[395] [13]
[396] “I should like to see them!” cried Cynthia with dignity.[397] [13]
[398] “I have got a cousin who is nursing,” I remarked. “And she is terrified[399] of ‘Sisters’.”[400] [13]
[401] “I don’t wonder. Sisters _are_, you know, Mr. Hastings. They simp-ly[402] _are_! You’ve no idea! But I’m not a nurse, thank heaven, I work in the[403] dispensary.”[404] [13]
[405] “How many people do you poison?” I asked, smiling.[406] [13]
[407] Cynthia smiled too.[408] [13]
[409] “Oh, hundreds!” she said.[410] [13]
[411] “Cynthia,” called Mrs. Inglethorp, “do you think you could write a few[412] notes for me?”[413] [13]
[414] “Certainly, Aunt Emily.”[415] [13]
[416] She jumped up promptly, and something in her manner reminded me that[417] her position was a dependent one, and that Mrs. Inglethorp, kind as she[418] might be in the main, did not allow her to forget it.[419] [13]
[420] My hostess turned to me.[421] [13]
[422] “John will show you your room. Supper is at half-past seven. We have[423] given up late dinner for some time now. Lady Tadminster, our Member’s[424] wife—she was the late Lord Abbotsbury’s daughter—does the same. She[425] agrees with me that one must set an example of economy. We are quite a[426] war household; nothing is wasted here—every scrap of waste paper, even,[427] is saved and sent away in sacks.”[428] [13]
[429] I expressed my appreciation, and John took me into the house and up the[430] broad staircase, which forked right and left half-way to different[431] wings of the building. My room was in the left wing, and looked out[432] over the park.[433] [13]
[434] John left me, and a few minutes later I saw him from my window walking[435] slowly across the grass arm in arm with Cynthia Murdoch. I heard Mrs.[436] Inglethorp call “Cynthia” impatiently, and the girl started and ran[437] back to the house. At the same moment, a man stepped out from the[438] shadow of a tree and walked slowly in the same direction. He looked[439] about forty, very dark with a melancholy clean-shaven face. Some[440] violent emotion seemed to be mastering him. He looked up at my window[441] as he passed, and I recognized him, though he had changed much in the[442] fifteen years that had elapsed since we last met. It was John’s younger[443] brother, Lawrence Cavendish. I wondered what it was that had brought[444] that singular expression to his face.[445] [13]
[446] Then I dismissed him from my mind, and returned to the contemplation of[447] my own affairs.[448] [13]
[449] The evening passed pleasantly enough; and I dreamed that night of that[450] enigmatical woman, Mary Cavendish.[451] [13]
[452] The next morning dawned bright and sunny, and I was full of the[453] anticipation of a delightful visit.[454] [13]
[455] I did not see Mrs. Cavendish until lunch-time, when she volunteered to[456] take me for a walk, and we spent a charming afternoon roaming in the[457] woods, returning to the house about five.[458] [13]
[459] As we entered the large hall, John beckoned us both into the[460] smoking-room. I saw at once by his face that something disturbing had[461] occurred. We followed him in, and he shut the door after us.[462] [13]
[463] “Look here, Mary, there’s the deuce of a mess. Evie’s had a row with[464] Alfred Inglethorp, and she’s off.”[465] [13]
[466] “Evie? Off?”[467] [13]
[468] John nodded gloomily.[469] [13]
[470] “Yes; you see she went to the mater, and—Oh,—here’s Evie herself.”[471] [13]
[472] Miss Howard entered. Her lips were set grimly together, and she carried[473] a small suit-case. She looked excited and determined, and slightly on[474] the defensive.[475] [13]
[476] “At any rate,” she burst out, “I’ve spoken my mind!”[477] [13]
[478] “My dear Evelyn,” cried Mrs. Cavendish, “this can’t be true!”[479] [13]
[480] Miss Howard nodded grimly.[481] [13]
[482] “True enough! Afraid I said some things to Emily she won’t forget or[483] forgive in a hurry. Don’t mind if they’ve only sunk in a bit. Probably[484] water off a duck’s back, though. I said right out: ‘You’re an old[485] woman, Emily, and there’s no fool like an old fool. The man’s twenty[486] years younger than you, and don’t you fool yourself as to what he[487] married you for. Money! Well, don’t let him have too much of it. Farmer[488] Raikes has got a very pretty young wife. Just ask your Alfred how much[489] time he spends over there.’ She was very angry. Natural! I went on,[490] ‘I’m going to warn you, whether you like it or not. That man would as[491] soon murder you in your bed as look at you. He’s a bad lot. You can say[492] what you like to me, but remember what I’ve told you. He’s a bad lot!’”[493] [13]
[494] “What did she say?”[495] [13]
[496] Miss Howard made an extremely expressive grimace.[497] [13]
[498] “‘Darling Alfred’—‘dearest Alfred’—‘wicked calumnies’ —‘wicked[499] lies’—‘wicked woman’—to accuse her ‘dear husband!’ The sooner I left[500] her house the better. So I’m off.”[501] [13]
[502] “But not now?”[503] [13]
[504] “This minute!”[505] [13]
[506] For a moment we sat and stared at her. Finally John Cavendish, finding[507] his persuasions of no avail, went off to look up the trains. His wife[508] followed him, murmuring something about persuading Mrs. Inglethorp to[509] think better of it.[510] [13]
[511] As she left the room, Miss Howard’s face changed. She leant towards me[512] eagerly.[513] [13]
[514] “Mr. Hastings, you’re honest. I can trust you?”[515] [13]
[516] I was a little startled. She laid her hand on my arm, and sank her[517] voice to a whisper.[518] [13]
[519] “Look after her, Mr. Hastings. My poor Emily. They’re a lot of[520] sharks—all of them. Oh, I know what I’m talking about. There isn’t one[521] of them that’s not hard up and trying to get money out of her. I’ve[522] protected her as much as I could. Now I’m out of the way, they’ll[523] impose upon her.”[524] [13]
[525] “Of course, Miss Howard,” I said, “I’ll do everything I can, but I’m[526] sure you’re excited and overwrought.”[527] [13]
[528] She interrupted me by slowly shaking her forefinger.[529] [13]
[530] “Young man, trust me. I’ve lived in the world rather longer than you[531] have. All I ask you is to keep your eyes open. You’ll see what I mean.”[532] [13]
[533] The throb of the motor came through the open window, and Miss Howard[534] rose and moved to the door. John’s voice sounded outside. With her hand[535] on the handle, she turned her head over her shoulder, and beckoned to[536] me.[537] [13]
[538] “Above all, Mr. Hastings, watch that devil—her husband!”[539] [13]
[540] There was no time for more. Miss Howard was swallowed up in an eager[541] chorus of protests and good-byes. The Inglethorps did not appear.[542] [13]
[543] As the motor drove away, Mrs. Cavendish suddenly detached herself from[544] the group, and moved across the drive to the lawn to meet a tall[545] bearded man who had been evidently making for the house. The colour[546] rose in her cheeks as she held out her hand to him.[547] [13]
[548] “Who is that?” I asked sharply, for instinctively I distrusted the man.[549] [13]
[550] “That’s Dr. Bauerstein,” said John shortly.[551] [13]
[552] “And who is Dr. Bauerstein?”[553] [13]
[554] “He’s staying in the village doing a rest cure, after a bad nervous[555] breakdown. He’s a London specialist; a very clever man—one of the[556] greatest living experts on poisons, I believe.”[557] [13]
[558] “And he’s a great friend of Mary’s,” put in Cynthia, the irrepressible.[559] [13]
[560] John Cavendish frowned and changed the subject.[561] [13]
[562] “Come for a stroll, Hastings. This has been a most rotten business. She[563] always had a rough tongue, but there is no stauncher friend in England[564] than Evelyn Howard.”[565] [13]
[566] He took the path through the plantation, and we walked down to the[567] village through the woods which bordered one side of the estate.[568] [13]
[569] As we passed through one of the gates on our way home again, a pretty[570] young woman of gipsy type coming in the opposite direction bowed and[571] smiled.[572] [13]
[573] “That’s a pretty girl,” I remarked appreciatively.[574] [13]
[575] John’s face hardened.[576] [13]
[577] “That is Mrs. Raikes.”[578] [13]
[579] “The one that Miss Howard——”[580] [13]
[581] “Exactly,” said John, with rather unnecessary abruptness.[582] [13]
[583] I thought of the white-haired old lady in the big house, and that vivid[584] wicked little face that had just smiled into ours, and a vague chill of[585] foreboding crept over me. I brushed it aside.[586] [13]
[587] “Styles is really a glorious old place,” I said to John.[588] [13]
[589] He nodded rather gloomily.[590] [13]
[591] “Yes, it’s a fine property. It’ll be mine some day—should be mine now[592] by rights, if my father had only made a decent will. And then I[593] shouldn’t be so damned hard up as I am now.”[594] [13]
[595] “Hard up, are you?”[596] [13]
[597] “My dear Hastings, I don’t mind telling you that I’m at my wits’ end[598] for money.”[599] [13]
[600] “Couldn’t your brother help you?”[601] [13]
[602] “Lawrence? He’s gone through every penny he ever had, publishing rotten[603] verses in fancy bindings. No, we’re an impecunious lot. My mother’s[604] always been awfully good to us, I must say. That is, up to now. Since[605] her marriage, of course——” he broke off, frowning.[606] [13]
[607] For the first time I felt that, with Evelyn Howard, something[608] indefinable had gone from the atmosphere. Her presence had spelt[609] security. Now that security was removed—and the air seemed rife with[610] suspicion. The sinister face of Dr. Bauerstein recurred to me[611] unpleasantly. A vague suspicion of everyone and everything filled my[612] mind. Just for a moment I had a premonition of approaching evil.[613] [13]
[614] [13]
[615] [13]
[616] [13]
[617] CHAPTER II.[618] THE 16TH AND 17TH OF JULY[619] [13]
[620] [13]
[621] I had arrived at Styles on the 5th of July. I come now to the events of[622] the 16th and 17th of that month. For the convenience of the reader I[623] will recapitulate the incidents of those days in as exact a manner as[624] possible. They were elicited subsequently at the trial by a process of[625] long and tedious cross-examinations.[626] [13]
[627] I received a letter from Evelyn Howard a couple of days after her[628] departure, telling me she was working as a nurse at the big hospital in[629] Middlingham, a manufacturing town some fifteen miles away, and begging[630] me to let her know if Mrs. Inglethorp should show any wish to be[631] reconciled.[632] [13]
[633] The only fly in the ointment of my peaceful days was Mrs. Cavendish’s[634] extraordinary, and, for my part, unaccountable preference for the[635] society of Dr. Bauerstein. What she saw in the man I cannot imagine,[636] but she was always asking him up to the house, and often went off for[637] long expeditions with him. I must confess that I was quite unable to[638] see his attraction.[639] [13]
[640] The 16th of July fell on a Monday. It was a day of turmoil. The famous[641] bazaar had taken place on Saturday, and an entertainment, in connection[642] with the same charity, at which Mrs. Inglethorp was to recite a War[643] poem, was to be held that night. We were all busy during the morning[644] arranging and decorating the Hall in the village where it was to take[645] place. We had a late luncheon and spent the afternoon resting in the[646] garden. I noticed that John’s manner was somewhat unusual. He seemed[647] very excited and restless.[648] [13]
[649] After tea, Mrs. Inglethorp went to lie down to rest before her efforts[650] in the evening and I challenged Mary Cavendish to a single at tennis.[651] [13]
[652] About a quarter to seven, Mrs. Inglethorp called us that we should be[653] late as supper was early that night. We had rather a scramble to get[654] ready in time; and before the meal was over the motor was waiting at[655] the door.[656] [13]
[657] The entertainment was a great success, Mrs. Inglethorp’s recitation[658] receiving tremendous applause. There were also some tableaux in which[659] Cynthia took part. She did not return with us, having been asked to a[660] supper party, and to remain the night with some friends who had been[661] acting with her in the tableaux.[662] [13]
[663] The following morning, Mrs. Inglethorp stayed in bed to breakfast, as[664] she was rather overtired; but she appeared in her briskest mood about[665] 12.30, and swept Lawrence and myself off to a luncheon party.[666] [13]
[667] “Such a charming invitation from Mrs. Rolleston. Lady Tadminster’s[668] sister, you know. The Rollestons came over with the Conqueror—one of[669] our oldest families.”[670] [13]
[671] Mary had excused herself on the plea of an engagement with Dr.[672] Bauerstein.[673] [13]
[674] We had a pleasant luncheon, and as we drove away Lawrence suggested[675] that we should return by Tadminster, which was barely a mile out of our[676] way, and pay a visit to Cynthia in her dispensary. Mrs. Inglethorp[677] replied that this was an excellent idea, but as she had several letters[678] to write she would drop us there, and we could come back with Cynthia[679] in the pony-trap.[680] [13]
[681] We were detained under suspicion by the hospital porter, until Cynthia[682] appeared to vouch for us, looking very cool and sweet in her long white[683] overall. She took us up to her sanctum, and introduced us to her fellow[684] dispenser, a rather awe-inspiring individual, whom Cynthia cheerily[685] addressed as “Nibs.”[686] [13]
[687] “What a lot of bottles!” I exclaimed, as my eye travelled round the[688] small room. “Do you really know what’s in them all?”[689] [13]
[690] “Say something original,” groaned Cynthia. “Every single person who[691] comes up here says that. We are really thinking of bestowing a prize on[692] the first individual who does _not_ say: ‘What a lot of bottles!’ And I[693] know the next thing you’re going to say is: ‘How many people have you[694] poisoned?’”[695] [13]
[696] I pleaded guilty with a laugh.[697] [13]
[698] “If you people only knew how fatally easy it is to poison someone by[699] mistake, you wouldn’t joke about it. Come on, let’s have tea. We’ve got[700] all sorts of secret stores in that cupboard. No, Lawrence—that’s the[701] poison cupboard. The big cupboard—that’s right.”[702] [13]
[703] We had a very cheery tea, and assisted Cynthia to wash up afterwards.[704] We had just put away the last tea-spoon when a knock came at the door.[705] The countenances of Cynthia and Nibs were suddenly petrified into a[706] stern and forbidding expression.[707] [13]
[708] “Come in,” said Cynthia, in a sharp professional tone.[709] [13]
[710] A young and rather scared looking nurse appeared with a bottle which[711] she proffered to Nibs, who waved her towards Cynthia with the somewhat[712] enigmatical remark:[713] [13]
[714] “_I_’m not really here to-day.”[715] [13]
[716] Cynthia took the bottle and examined it with the severity of a judge.[717] [13]
[718] “This should have been sent up this morning.”[719] [13]
[720] “Sister is very sorry. She forgot.”[721] [13]
[722] “Sister should read the rules outside the door.”[723] [13]
[724] I gathered from the little nurse’s expression that there was not the[725] least likelihood of her having the hardihood to retail this message to[726] the dreaded “Sister”.[727] [13]
[728] “So now it can’t be done until to-morrow,” finished Cynthia.[729] [13]
[730] “Don’t you think you could possibly let us have it to-night?”[731] [13]
[732] “Well,” said Cynthia graciously, “we are very busy, but if we have time[733] it shall be done.”[734] [13]
[735] The little nurse withdrew, and Cynthia promptly took a jar from the[736] shelf, refilled the bottle, and placed it on the table outside the[737] door.[738] [13]
[739] I laughed.[740] [13]
[741] “Discipline must be maintained?”[742] [13]
[743] “Exactly. Come out on our little balcony. You can see all the outside[744] wards there.”[745] [13]
[746] I followed Cynthia and her friend and they pointed out the different[747] wards to me. Lawrence remained behind, but after a few moments Cynthia[748] called to him over her shoulder to come and join us. Then she looked at[749] her watch.[750] [13]
[751] “Nothing more to do, Nibs?”[752] [13]
[753] “No.”[754] [13]
[755] “All right. Then we can lock up and go.”[756] [13]
[757] I had seen Lawrence in quite a different light that afternoon. Compared[758] to John, he was an astoundingly difficult person to get to know. He was[759] the opposite of his brother in almost every respect, being unusually[760] shy and reserved. Yet he had a certain charm of manner, and I fancied[761] that, if one really knew him well, one could have a deep affection for[762] him. I had always fancied that his manner to Cynthia was rather[763] constrained, and that she on her side was inclined to be shy of him.[764] But they were both gay enough this afternoon, and chatted together like[765] a couple of children.[766] [13]
[767] As we drove through the village, I remembered that I wanted some[768] stamps, so accordingly we pulled up at the post office.[769] [13]
[770] As I came out again, I cannoned into a little man who was just[771] entering. I drew aside and apologised, when suddenly, with a loud[772] exclamation, he clasped me in his arms and kissed me warmly.[773] [13]
[774] “_Mon ami_ Hastings!” he cried. “It is indeed _mon ami_ Hastings!”[775] [13]
[776] “Poirot!” I exclaimed.[777] [13]
[778] I turned to the pony-trap.[779] [13]
[780] “This is a very pleasant meeting for me, Miss Cynthia. This is my old[781] friend, Monsieur Poirot, whom I have not seen for years.”[782] [13]
[783] “Oh, we know Monsieur Poirot,” said Cynthia gaily. “But I had no idea[784] he was a friend of yours.”[785] [13]
[786] “Yes, indeed,” said Poirot seriously. “I know Mademoiselle Cynthia. It[787] is by the charity of that good Mrs. Inglethorp that I am here.” Then,[788] as I looked at him inquiringly: “Yes, my friend, she had kindly[789] extended hospitality to seven of my countrypeople who, alas, are[790] refugees from their native land. We Belgians will always remember her[791] with gratitude.”[792] [13]
[793] Poirot was an extraordinary looking little man. He was hardly more than[794] five feet, four inches, but carried himself with great dignity. His[795] head was exactly the shape of an egg, and he always perched it a little[796] on one side. His moustache was very stiff and military. The neatness of[797] his attire was almost incredible. I believe a speck of dust would have[798] caused him more pain than a bullet wound. Yet this quaint dandified[799] little man who, I was sorry to see, now limped badly, had been in his[800] time one of the most celebrated members of the Belgian police. As a[801] detective, his _flair_ had been extraordinary, and he had achieved[802] triumphs by unravelling some of the most baffling cases of the day.[803] [13]
[804] He pointed out to me the little house inhabited by him and his fellow[805] Belgians, and I promised to go and see him at an early date. Then he[806] raised his hat with a flourish to Cynthia, and we drove away.[807] [13]
[808] “He’s a dear little man,” said Cynthia. “I’d no idea you knew him.”[809] [13]
[810] “You’ve been entertaining a celebrity unawares,” I replied.[811] [13]
[812] And, for the rest of the way home, I recited to them the various[813] exploits and triumphs of Hercule Poirot.[814] [13]
[815] We arrived back in a very cheerful mood. As we entered the hall, Mrs.[816] Inglethorp came out of her boudoir. She looked flushed and upset.[817] [13]
[818] “Oh, it’s you,” she said.[819] [13]
[820] “Is there anything the matter, Aunt Emily?” asked Cynthia.[821] [13]
[822] “Certainly not,” said Mrs. Inglethorp sharply. “What should there be?”[823] Then catching sight of Dorcas, the parlourmaid, going into the[824] dining-room, she called to her to bring some stamps into the boudoir.[825] [13]
[826] “Yes, m’m.” The old servant hesitated, then added diffidently: “Don’t[827] you think, m’m, you’d better get to bed? You’re looking very tired.”[828] [13]
[829] “Perhaps you’re right, Dorcas—yes—no—not now. I’ve some letters I must[830] finish by post-time. Have you lighted the fire in my room as I told[831] you?”[832] [13]
[833] “Yes, m’m.”[834] [13]
[835] “Then I’ll go to bed directly after supper.”[836] [13]
[837] She went into the boudoir again, and Cynthia stared after her.[838] [13]
[839] “Goodness gracious! I wonder what’s up?” she said to Lawrence.[840] [13]
[841] He did not seem to have heard her, for without a word he turned on his[842] heel and went out of the house.[843] [13]
[844] I suggested a quick game of tennis before supper and, Cynthia agreeing,[845] I ran upstairs to fetch my racquet.[846] [13]
[847] Mrs. Cavendish was coming down the stairs. It may have been my fancy,[848] but she, too, was looking odd and disturbed.[849] [13]
[850] “Had a good walk with Dr. Bauerstein?” I asked, trying to appear as[851] indifferent as I could.[852] [13]
[853] “I didn’t go,” she replied abruptly. “Where is Mrs. Inglethorp?”[854] [13]
[855] “In the boudoir.”[856] [13]
[857] Her hand clenched itself on the banisters, then she seemed to nerve[858] herself for some encounter, and went rapidly past me down the stairs[859] across the hall to the boudoir, the door of which she shut behind her.[860] [13]
[861] As I ran out to the tennis court a few moments later, I had to pass the[862] open boudoir window, and was unable to help overhearing the following[863] scrap of dialogue. Mary Cavendish was saying in the voice of a woman[864] desperately controlling herself:[865] [13]
[866] “Then you won’t show it to me?”[867] [13]
[868] To which Mrs. Inglethorp replied:[869] [13]
[870] “My dear Mary, it has nothing to do with that matter.”[871] [13]
[872] “Then show it to me.”[873] [13]
[874] “I tell you it is not what you imagine. It does not concern you in the[875] least.”[876] [13]
[877] To which Mary Cavendish replied, with a rising bitterness:[878] [13]
[879] “Of course, I might have known you would shield him.”[880] [13]
[881] Cynthia was waiting for me, and greeted me eagerly with:[882] [13]
[883] “I say! There’s been the most awful row! I’ve got it all out of[884] Dorcas.”[885] [13]
[886] “What kind of a row?”[887] [13]
[888] “Between Aunt Emily and _him_. I do hope she’s found him out at last!”[889] [13]
[890] “Was Dorcas there, then?”[891] [13]
[892] “Of course not. She ‘happened to be near the door’. It was a real old[893] bust-up. I do wish I knew what it was all about.”[894] [13]
[895] I thought of Mrs. Raikes’s gipsy face, and Evelyn Howard’s warnings,[896] but wisely decided to hold my peace, whilst Cynthia exhausted every[897] possible hypothesis, and cheerfully hoped, “Aunt Emily will send him[898] away, and will never speak to him again.”[899] [13]
[900] I was anxious to get hold of John, but he was nowhere to be seen.[901] Evidently something very momentous had occurred that afternoon. I tried[902] to forget the few words I had overheard; but, do what I would, I could[903] not dismiss them altogether from my mind. What was Mary Cavendish’s[904] concern in the matter?[905] [13]
[906] Mr. Inglethorp was in the drawing-room when I came down to supper. His[907] face was impassive as ever, and the strange unreality of the man struck[908] me afresh.[909] [13]
[910] Mrs. Inglethorp came down last. She still looked agitated, and during[911] the meal there was a somewhat constrained silence. Inglethorp was[912] unusually quiet. As a rule, he surrounded his wife with little[913] attentions, placing a cushion at her back, and altogether playing the[914] part of the devoted husband. Immediately after supper, Mrs. Inglethorp[915] retired to her boudoir again.[916] [13]
[917] “Send my coffee in here, Mary,” she called. “I’ve just five minutes to[918] catch the post.”[919] [13]
[920] Cynthia and I went and sat by the open window in the drawing-room. Mary[921] Cavendish brought our coffee to us. She seemed excited.[922] [13]
[923] “Do you young people want lights, or do you enjoy the twilight?” she[924] asked. “Will you take Mrs. Inglethorp her coffee, Cynthia? I will pour[925] it out.”[926] [13]
[927] “Do not trouble, Mary,” said Inglethorp. “I will take it to Emily.” He[928] poured it out, and went out of the room carrying it carefully.[929] [13]
[930] Lawrence followed him, and Mrs. Cavendish sat down by us.[931] [13]
[932] We three sat for some time in silence. It was a glorious night, hot and[933] still. Mrs. Cavendish fanned herself gently with a palm leaf.[934] [13]
[935] “It’s almost too hot,” she murmured. “We shall have a thunderstorm.”[936] [13]
[937] Alas, that these harmonious moments can never endure! My paradise was[938] rudely shattered by the sound of a well known, and heartily disliked,[939] voice in the hall.[940] [13]
[941] “Dr. Bauerstein!” exclaimed Cynthia. “What a funny time to come.”[942] [13]
[943] I glanced jealously at Mary Cavendish, but she seemed quite[944] undisturbed, the delicate pallor of her cheeks did not vary.[945] [13]
[946] In a few moments, Alfred Inglethorp had ushered the doctor in, the[947] latter laughing, and protesting that he was in no fit state for a[948] drawing-room. In truth, he presented a sorry spectacle, being literally[949] plastered with mud.[950] [13]
[951] “What have you been doing, doctor?” cried Mrs. Cavendish.[952] [13]
[953] “I must make my apologies,” said the doctor. “I did not really mean to[954] come in, but Mr. Inglethorp insisted.”[955] [13]
[956] “Well, Bauerstein, you are in a plight,” said John, strolling in from[957] the hall. “Have some coffee, and tell us what you have been up to.”[958] [13]
[959] “Thank you, I will.” He laughed rather ruefully, as he described how he[960] had discovered a very rare species of fern in an inaccessible place,[961] and in his efforts to obtain it had lost his footing, and slipped[962] ignominiously into a neighbouring pond.[963] [13]
[964] “The sun soon dried me off,” he added, “but I’m afraid my appearance is[965] very disreputable.”[966] [13]
[967] At this juncture, Mrs. Inglethorp called to Cynthia from the hall, and[968] the girl ran out.[969] [13]
[970] “Just carry up my despatch-case, will you, dear? I’m going to bed.”[971] [13]
[972] The door into the hall was a wide one. I had risen when Cynthia did,[973] John was close by me. There were therefore three witnesses who could[974] swear that Mrs. Inglethorp was carrying her coffee, as yet untasted, in[975] her hand.[976] [13]
[977] My evening was utterly and entirely spoilt by the presence of Dr.[978] Bauerstein. It seemed to me the man would never go. He rose at last,[979] however, and I breathed a sigh of relief.[980] [13]
[981] “I’ll walk down to the village with you,” said Mr. Inglethorp. “I must[982] see our agent over those estate accounts.” He turned to John. “No one[983] need sit up. I will take the latch-key.”[984] [13]
[985] [13]
[986] [13]
[987] [13]
[988] CHAPTER III.[989] THE NIGHT OF THE TRAGEDY[990] [13]
[991] [13]
[992] To make this part of my story clear, I append the following plan of the[993] first floor of Styles. The servants’ rooms are reached through the door[994] B. They have no communication with the right wing, where the[995] Inglethorps’ rooms were situated.[996] [13]
[997] [Illustration][998] [13]
[999] It seemed to be the middle of the night when I was awakened by Lawrence[1000] Cavendish. He had a candle in his hand, and the agitation of his face[1001] told me at once that something was seriously wrong.[1002] [13]
[1003] “What’s the matter?” I asked, sitting up in bed, and trying to collect[1004] my scattered thoughts.[1005] [13]
[1006] “We are afraid my mother is very ill. She seems to be having some kind[1007] of fit. Unfortunately she has locked herself in.”[1008] [13]
[1009] “I’ll come at once.”[1010] [13]
[1011] I sprang out of bed; and, pulling on a dressing-gown, followed Lawrence[1012] along the passage and the gallery to the right wing of the house.[1013] [13]
[1014] John Cavendish joined us, and one or two of the servants were standing[1015] round in a state of awe-stricken excitement. Lawrence turned to his[1016] brother.[1017] [13]
[1018] “What do you think we had better do?”[1019] [13]
[1020] Never, I thought, had his indecision of character been more apparent.[1021] [13]
[1022] John rattled the handle of Mrs. Inglethorp’s door violently, but with[1023] no effect. It was obviously locked or bolted on the inside. The whole[1024] household was aroused by now. The most alarming sounds were audible[1025] from the interior of the room. Clearly something must be done.[1026] [13]
[1027] “Try going through Mr. Inglethorp’s room, sir,” cried Dorcas. “Oh, the[1028] poor mistress!”[1029] [13]
[1030] Suddenly I realized that Alfred Inglethorp was not with us—that he[1031] alone had given no sign of his presence. John opened the door of his[1032] room. It was pitch dark, but Lawrence was following with the candle,[1033] and by its feeble light we saw that the bed had not been slept in, and[1034] that there was no sign of the room having been occupied.[1035] [13]
[1036] We went straight to the connecting door. That, too, was locked or[1037] bolted on the inside. What was to be done?[1038] [13]
[1039] “Oh, dear, sir,” cried Dorcas, wringing her hands, “what ever shall we[1040] do?”[1041] [13]
[1042] “We must try and break the door in, I suppose. It’ll be a tough job,[1043] though. Here, let one of the maids go down and wake Baily and tell him[1044] to go for Dr. Wilkins at once. Now then, we’ll have a try at the door.[1045] Half a moment, though, isn’t there a door into Miss Cynthia’s rooms?”[1046] [13]
[1047] “Yes, sir, but that’s always bolted. It’s never been undone.”[1048] [13]
[1049] “Well, we might just see.”[1050] [13]
[1051] He ran rapidly down the corridor to Cynthia’s room. Mary Cavendish was[1052] there, shaking the girl—who must have been an unusually sound[1053] sleeper—and trying to wake her.[1054] [13]
[1055] In a moment or two he was back.[1056] [13]
[1057] “No good. That’s bolted too. We must break in the door. I think this[1058] one is a shade less solid than the one in the passage.”[1059] [13]
[1060] We strained and heaved together. The framework of the door was solid,[1061] and for a long time it resisted our efforts, but at last we felt it[1062] give beneath our weight, and finally, with a resounding crash, it was[1063] burst open.[1064] [13]
[1065] We stumbled in together, Lawrence still holding his candle. Mrs.[1066] Inglethorp was lying on the bed, her whole form agitated by violent[1067] convulsions, in one of which she must have overturned the table beside[1068] her. As we entered, however, her limbs relaxed, and she fell back upon[1069] the pillows.[1070] [13]
[1071] John strode across the room, and lit the gas. Turning to Annie, one of[1072] the housemaids, he sent her downstairs to the dining-room for brandy.[1073] Then he went across to his mother whilst I unbolted the door that gave[1074] on the corridor.[1075] [13]
[1076] I turned to Lawrence, to suggest that I had better leave them now that[1077] there was no further need of my services, but the words were frozen on[1078] my lips. Never have I seen such a ghastly look on any man’s face. He[1079] was white as chalk, the candle he held in his shaking hand was[1080] sputtering onto the carpet, and his eyes, petrified with terror, or[1081] some such kindred emotion, stared fixedly over my head at a point on[1082] the further wall. It was as though he had seen something that turned[1083] him to stone. I instinctively followed the direction of his eyes, but I[1084] could see nothing unusual. The still feebly flickering ashes in the[1085] grate, and the row of prim ornaments on the mantelpiece, were surely[1086] harmless enough.[1087] [13]
[1088] The violence of Mrs. Inglethorp’s attack seemed to be passing. She was[1089] able to speak in short gasps.[1090] [13]
[1091] “Better now—very sudden—stupid of me—to lock myself in.”[1092] [13]
[1093] A shadow fell on the bed and, looking up, I saw Mary Cavendish standing[1094] near the door with her arm around Cynthia. She seemed to be supporting[1095] the girl, who looked utterly dazed and unlike herself. Her face was[1096] heavily flushed, and she yawned repeatedly.[1097] [13]
[1098] “Poor Cynthia is quite frightened,” said Mrs. Cavendish in a low clear[1099] voice. She herself, I noticed, was dressed in her white land smock.[1100] Then it must be later than I thought. I saw that a faint streak of[1101] daylight was showing through the curtains of the windows, and that the[1102] clock on the mantelpiece pointed to close upon five o’clock.[1103] [13]
[1104] A strangled cry from the bed startled me. A fresh access of pain seized[1105] the unfortunate old lady. The convulsions were of a violence terrible[1106] to behold. Everything was confusion. We thronged round her, powerless[1107] to help or alleviate. A final convulsion lifted her from the bed, until[1108] she appeared to rest upon her head and her heels, with her body arched[1109] in an extraordinary manner. In vain Mary and John tried to administer[1110] more brandy. The moments flew. Again the body arched itself in that[1111] peculiar fashion.[1112] [13]
[1113] At that moment, Dr. Bauerstein pushed his way authoritatively into the[1114] room. For one instant he stopped dead, staring at the figure on the[1115] bed, and, at the same instant, Mrs. Inglethorp cried out in a strangled[1116] voice, her eyes fixed on the doctor:[1117] [13]
[1118] “Alfred—Alfred——” Then she fell back motionless on the pillows.[1119] [13]
[1120] With a stride, the doctor reached the bed, and seizing her arms worked[1121] them energetically, applying what I knew to be artificial respiration.[1122] He issued a few short sharp orders to the servants. An imperious wave[1123] of his hand drove us all to the door. We watched him, fascinated,[1124] though I think we all knew in our hearts that it was too late, and that[1125] nothing could be done now. I could see by the expression on his face[1126] that he himself had little hope.[1127] [13]
[1128] Finally he abandoned his task, shaking his head gravely. At that[1129] moment, we heard footsteps outside, and Dr. Wilkins, Mrs. Inglethorp’s[1130] own doctor, a portly, fussy little man, came bustling in.[1131] [13]
[1132] In a few words Dr. Bauerstein explained how he had happened to be[1133] passing the lodge gates as the car came out, and had run up to the[1134] house as fast as he could, whilst the car went on to fetch Dr. Wilkins.[1135] With a faint gesture of the hand, he indicated the figure on the bed.[1136] [13]
[1137] “Ve—ry sad. Ve—ry sad,” murmured Dr. Wilkins. “Poor dear lady. Always[1138] did far too much—far too much—against my advice. I warned her. Her[1139] heart was far from strong. ‘Take it easy,’ I said to her,[1140] ‘Take—it—easy’. But no—her zeal for good works was too great. Nature[1141] rebelled. Na—ture—re—belled.”[1142] [13]
[1143] Dr. Bauerstein, I noticed, was watching the local doctor narrowly. He[1144] still kept his eyes fixed on him as he spoke.[1145] [13]
[1146] “The convulsions were of a peculiar violence, Dr. Wilkins. I am sorry[1147] you were not here in time to witness them. They were quite—tetanic in[1148] character.”[1149] [13]
[1150] “Ah!” said Dr. Wilkins wisely.[1151] [13]
[1152] “I should like to speak to you in private,” said Dr. Bauerstein. He[1153] turned to John. “You do not object?”[1154] [13]
[1155] “Certainly not.”[1156] [13]
[1157] We all trooped out into the corridor, leaving the two doctors alone,[1158] and I heard the key turned in the lock behind us.[1159] [13]
[1160] We went slowly down the stairs. I was violently excited. I have a[1161] certain talent for deduction, and Dr. Bauerstein’s manner had started a[1162] flock of wild surmises in my mind. Mary Cavendish laid her hand upon my[1163] arm.[1164] [13]
[1165] “What is it? Why did Dr. Bauerstein seem so—peculiar?”[1166] [13]
[1167] I looked at her.[1168] [13]
[1169] “Do you know what I think?”[1170] [13]
[1171] “What?”[1172] [13]
[1173] “Listen!” I looked round, the others were out of earshot. I lowered my[1174] voice to a whisper. “I believe she has been poisoned! I’m certain Dr.[1175] Bauerstein suspects it.”[1176] [13]
[1177] “_What?_” She shrank against the wall, the pupils of her eyes dilating[1178] wildly. Then, with a sudden cry that startled me, she cried out: “No,[1179] no—not that—not that!” And breaking from me, fled up the stairs. I[1180] followed her, afraid that she was going to faint. I found her leaning[1181] against the bannisters, deadly pale. She waved me away impatiently.[1182] [13]
[1183] “No, no—leave me. I’d rather be alone. Let me just be quiet for a[1184] minute or two. Go down to the others.”[1185] [13]
[1186] I obeyed her reluctantly. John and Lawrence were in the dining-room. I[1187] joined them. We were all silent, but I suppose I voiced the thoughts of[1188] us all when I at last broke it by saying:[1189] [13]
[1190] “Where is Mr. Inglethorp?”[1191] [13]
[1192] John shook his head.[1193] [13]
[1194] “He’s not in the house.”[1195] [13]
[1196] Our eyes met. Where _was_ Alfred Inglethorp? His absence was strange[1197] and inexplicable. I remembered Mrs. Inglethorp’s dying words. What lay[1198] beneath them? What more could she have told us, if she had had time?[1199] [13]
[1200] At last we heard the doctors descending the stairs. Dr. Wilkins was[1201] looking important and excited, and trying to conceal an inward[1202] exultation under a manner of decorous calm. Dr. Bauerstein remained in[1203] the background, his grave bearded face unchanged. Dr. Wilkins was the[1204] spokesman for the two. He addressed himself to John:[1205] [13]
[1206] “Mr. Cavendish, I should like your consent to a post-mortem.”[1207] [13]
[1208] “Is that necessary?” asked John gravely. A spasm of pain crossed his[1209] face.[1210] [13]
[1211] “Absolutely,” said Dr. Bauerstein.[1212] [13]
[1213] “You mean by that——?”[1214] [13]
[1215] “That neither Dr. Wilkins nor myself could give a death certificate[1216] under the circumstances.”[1217] [13]
[1218] John bent his head.[1219] [13]
[1220] “In that case, I have no alternative but to agree.”[1221] [13]
[1222] “Thank you,” said Dr. Wilkins briskly. “We propose that it should take[1223] place to-morrow night—or rather to-night.” And he glanced at the[1224] daylight. “Under the circumstances, I am afraid an inquest can hardly[1225] be avoided—these formalities are necessary, but I beg that you won’t[1226] distress yourselves.”[1227] [13]
[1228] There was a pause, and then Dr. Bauerstein drew two keys from his[1229] pocket, and handed them to John.[1230] [13]
[1231] “These are the keys of the two rooms. I have locked them and, in my[1232] opinion, they would be better kept locked for the present.”[1233] [13]
[1234] The doctors then departed.[1235] [13]
[1236] I had been turning over an idea in my head, and I felt that the moment[1237] had now come to broach it. Yet I was a little chary of doing so. John,[1238] I knew, had a horror of any kind of publicity, and was an easygoing[1239] optimist, who preferred never to meet trouble half-way. It might be[1240] difficult to convince him of the soundness of my plan. Lawrence, on the[1241] other hand, being less conventional, and having more imagination, I[1242] felt I might count upon as an ally. There was no doubt that the moment[1243] had come for me to take the lead.[1244] [13]
[1245] “John,” I said, “I am going to ask you something.”[1246] [13]
[1247] “Well?”[1248] [13]
[1249] “You remember my speaking of my friend Poirot? The Belgian who is here?[1250] He has been a most famous detective.”[1251] [13]
[1252] “Yes.”[1253] [13]
[1254] “I want you to let me call him in—to investigate this matter.”[1255] [13]
[1256] “What—now? Before the post-mortem?”[1257] [13]
[1258] “Yes, time is an advantage if—if—there has been foul play.”[1259] [13]
[1260] “Rubbish!” cried Lawrence angrily. “In my opinion the whole thing is a[1261] mare’s nest of Bauerstein’s! Wilkins hadn’t an idea of such a thing,[1262] until Bauerstein put it into his head. But, like all specialists,[1263] Bauerstein’s got a bee in his bonnet. Poisons are his hobby, so of[1264] course he sees them everywhere.”[1265] [13]
[1266] I confess that I was surprised by Lawrence’s attitude. He was so seldom[1267] vehement about anything.[1268] [13]
[1269] John hesitated.[1270] [13]
[1271] “I can’t feel as you do, Lawrence,” he said at last. “I’m inclined to[1272] give Hastings a free hand, though I should prefer to wait a bit. We[1273] don’t want any unnecessary scandal.”[1274] [13]
[1275] “No, no,” I cried eagerly, “you need have no fear of that. Poirot is[1276] discretion itself.”[1277] [13]
[1278] “Very well, then, have it your own way. I leave it in your hands.[1279] Though, if it is as we suspect, it seems a clear enough case. God[1280] forgive me if I am wronging him!”[1281] [13]
[1282] I looked at my watch. It was six o’clock. I determined to lose no time.[1283] [13]
[1284] Five minutes’ delay, however, I allowed myself. I spent it in[1285] ransacking the library until I discovered a medical book which gave a[1286] description of strychnine poisoning.[1287] [13]
[1288] [13]
[1289] [13]
[1290] [13]
[1291] CHAPTER IV.[1292] POIROT INVESTIGATES[1293] [13]
[1294] [13]
[1295] The house which the Belgians occupied in the village was quite close to[1296] the park gates. One could save time by taking a narrow path through the[1297] long grass, which cut off the detours of the winding drive. So I,[1298] accordingly, went that way. I had nearly reached the lodge, when my[1299] attention was arrested by the running figure of a man approaching me.[1300] It was Mr. Inglethorp. Where had he been? How did he intend to explain[1301] his absence?[1302] [13]
[1303] He accosted me eagerly.[1304] [13]
[1305] “My God! This is terrible! My poor wife! I have only just heard.”[1306] [13]
[1307] “Where have you been?” I asked.[1308] [13]
[1309] “Denby kept me late last night. It was one o’clock before we’d[1310] finished. Then I found that I’d forgotten the latch-key after all. I[1311] didn’t want to arouse the household, so Denby gave me a bed.”[1312] [13]
[1313] “How did you hear the news?” I asked.[1314] [13]
[1315] “Wilkins knocked Denby up to tell him. My poor Emily! She was so[1316] self-sacrificing—such a noble character. She over-taxed her strength.”[1317] [13]
[1318] A wave of revulsion swept over me. What a consummate hypocrite the man[1319] was![1320] [13]
[1321] “I must hurry on,” I said, thankful that he did not ask me whither I[1322] was bound.[1323] [13]
[1324] In a few minutes I was knocking at the door of Leastways Cottage.[1325] [13]
[1326] Getting no answer, I repeated my summons impatiently. A window above me[1327] was cautiously opened, and Poirot himself looked out.[1328] [13]
[1329] He gave an exclamation of surprise at seeing me. In a few brief words,[1330] I explained the tragedy that had occurred, and that I wanted his help.[1331] [13]
[1332] “Wait, my friend, I will let you in, and you shall recount to me the[1333] affair whilst I dress.”[1334] [13]
[1335] In a few moments he had unbarred the door, and I followed him up to his[1336] room. There he installed me in a chair, and I related the whole story,[1337] keeping back nothing, and omitting no circumstance, however[1338] insignificant, whilst he himself made a careful and deliberate toilet.[1339] [13]
[1340] I told him of my awakening, of Mrs. Inglethorp’s dying words, of her[1341] husband’s absence, of the quarrel the day before, of the scrap of[1342] conversation between Mary and her mother-in-law that I had overheard,[1343] of the former quarrel between Mrs. Inglethorp and Evelyn Howard, and of[1344] the latter’s innuendoes.[1345] [13]
[1346] I was hardly as clear as I could wish. I repeated myself several times,[1347] and occasionally had to go back to some detail that I had forgotten.[1348] Poirot smiled kindly on me.[1349] [13]
[1350] “The mind is confused? Is it not so? Take time, _mon ami_. You are[1351] agitated; you are excited—it is but natural. Presently, when we are[1352] calmer, we will arrange the facts, neatly, each in his proper place. We[1353] will examine—and reject. Those of importance we will put on one side;[1354] those of no importance, pouf!”—he screwed up his cherub-like face, and[1355] puffed comically enough—“blow them away!”[1356] [13]
[1357] “That’s all very well,” I objected, “but how are you going to decide[1358] what is important, and what isn’t? That always seems the difficulty to[1359] me.”[1360] [13]
[1361] Poirot shook his head energetically. He was now arranging his moustache[1362] with exquisite care.[1363] [13]
[1364] “Not so. _Voyons!_ One fact leads to another—so we continue. Does the[1365] next fit in with that? _A merveille!_ Good! We can proceed. This next[1366] little fact—no! Ah, that is curious! There is something missing—a link[1367] in the chain that is not there. We examine. We search. And that little[1368] curious fact, that possibly paltry little detail that will not tally,[1369] we put it here!” He made an extravagant gesture with his hand. “It is[1370] significant! It is tremendous!”[1371] [13]
[1372] “Y—es——”[1373] [13]
[1374] “Ah!” Poirot shook his forefinger so fiercely at me that I quailed[1375] before it. “Beware! Peril to the detective who says: ‘It is so small—it[1376] does not matter. It will not agree. I will forget it.’ That way lies[1377] confusion! Everything matters.”[1378] [13]
[1379] “I know. You always told me that. That’s why I have gone into all the[1380] details of this thing whether they seemed to me relevant or not.”[1381] [13]
[1382] “And I am pleased with you. You have a good memory, and you have given[1383] me the facts faithfully. Of the order in which you present them, I say[1384] nothing—truly, it is deplorable! But I make allowances—you are upset.[1385] To that I attribute the circumstance that you have omitted one fact of[1386] paramount importance.”[1387] [13]
[1388] “What is that?” I asked.[1389] [13]
[1390] “You have not told me if Mrs. Inglethorp ate well last night.”[1391] [13]
[1392] I stared at him. Surely the war had affected the little man’s brain. He[1393] was carefully engaged in brushing his coat before putting it on, and[1394] seemed wholly engrossed in the task.[1395] [13]
[1396] “I don’t remember,” I said. “And, anyway, I don’t see——”[1397] [13]
[1398] “You do not see? But it is of the first importance.”[1399] [13]
[1400] “I can’t see why,” I said, rather nettled. “As far as I can remember,[1401] she didn’t eat much. She was obviously upset, and it had taken her[1402] appetite away. That was only natural.”[1403] [13]
[1404] “Yes,” said Poirot thoughtfully, “it was only natural.”[1405] [13]
[1406] He opened a drawer, and took out a small despatch-case, then turned to[1407] me.[1408] [13]
[1409] “Now I am ready. We will proceed to the château, and study matters on[1410] the spot. Excuse me, _mon ami_, you dressed in haste, and your tie is[1411] on one side. Permit me.” With a deft gesture, he rearranged it.[1412] [13]
[1413] “_Ça y est!_ Now, shall we start?”[1414] [13]
[1415] We hurried up the village, and turned in at the lodge gates. Poirot[1416] stopped for a moment, and gazed sorrowfully over the beautiful expanse[1417] of park, still glittering with morning dew.[1418] [13]
[1419] “So beautiful, so beautiful, and yet, the poor family, plunged in[1420] sorrow, prostrated with grief.”[1421] [13]
[1422] He looked at me keenly as he spoke, and I was aware that I reddened[1423] under his prolonged gaze.[1424] [13]
[1425] Was the family prostrated by grief? Was the sorrow at Mrs. Inglethorp’s[1426] death so great? I realized that there was an emotional lack in the[1427] atmosphere. The dead woman had not the gift of commanding love. Her[1428] death was a shock and a distress, but she would not be passionately[1429] regretted.[1430] [13]
[1431] Poirot seemed to follow my thoughts. He nodded his head gravely.[1432] [13]
[1433] “No, you are right,” he said, “it is not as though there was a blood[1434] tie. She has been kind and generous to these Cavendishes, but she was[1435] not their own mother. Blood tells—always remember that—blood tells.”[1436] [13]
[1437] “Poirot,” I said, “I wish you would tell me why you wanted to know if[1438] Mrs. Inglethorp ate well last night? I have been turning it over in my[1439] mind, but I can’t see how it has anything to do with the matter?”[1440] [13]
[1441] He was silent for a minute or two as we walked along, but finally he[1442] said:[1443] [13]
[1444] “I do not mind telling you—though, as you know, it is not my habit to[1445] explain until the end is reached. The present contention is that Mrs.[1446] Inglethorp died of strychnine poisoning, presumably administered in her[1447] coffee.”[1448] [13]
[1449] “Yes?”[1450] [13]
[1451] “Well, what time was the coffee served?”[1452] [13]
[1453] “About eight o’clock.”[1454] [13]
[1455] “Therefore she drank it between then and half-past eight—certainly not[1456] much later. Well, strychnine is a fairly rapid poison. Its effects[1457] would be felt very soon, probably in about an hour. Yet, in Mrs.[1458] Inglethorp’s case, the symptoms do not manifest themselves until five[1459] o’clock the next morning: nine hours! But a heavy meal, taken at about[1460] the same time as the poison, might retard its effects, though hardly to[1461] that extent. Still, it is a possibility to be taken into account. But,[1462] according to you, she ate very little for supper, and yet the symptoms[1463] do not develop until early the next morning! Now that is a curious[1464] circumstance, my friend. Something may arise at the autopsy to explain[1465] it. In the meantime, remember it.”[1466] [13]
[1467] As we neared the house, John came out and met us. His face looked weary[1468] and haggard.[1469] [13]
[1470] “This is a very dreadful business, Monsieur Poirot,” he said. “Hastings[1471] has explained to you that we are anxious for no publicity?”[1472] [13]
[1473] “I comprehend perfectly.”[1474] [13]
[1475] “You see, it is only suspicion so far. We have nothing to go upon.”[1476] [13]
[1477] “Precisely. It is a matter of precaution only.”[1478] [13]
[1479] John turned to me, taking out his cigarette-case, and lighting a[1480] cigarette as he did so.[1481] [13]
[1482] “You know that fellow Inglethorp is back?”[1483] [13]
[1484] “Yes. I met him.”[1485] [13]
[1486] John flung the match into an adjacent flower bed, a proceeding which[1487] was too much for Poirot’s feelings. He retrieved it, and buried it[1488] neatly.[1489] [13]
[1490] “It’s jolly difficult to know how to treat him.”[1491] [13]
[1492] “That difficulty will not exist long,” pronounced Poirot quietly.[1493] [13]
[1494] John looked puzzled, not quite understanding the portent of this[1495] cryptic saying. He handed the two keys which Dr. Bauerstein had given[1496] him to me.[1497] [13]
[1498] “Show Monsieur Poirot everything he wants to see.”[1499] [13]
[1500] “The rooms are locked?” asked Poirot.[1501] [13]
[1502] “Dr. Bauerstein considered it advisable.”[1503] [13]
[1504] Poirot nodded thoughtfully.[1505] [13]
[1506] “Then he is very sure. Well, that simplifies matters for us.”[1507] [13]
[1508] We went up together to the room of the tragedy. For convenience I[1509] append a plan of the room and the principal articles of furniture in[1510] it.[1511] [13]
[1512] [Illustration][1513] [13]
[1514] Poirot locked the door on the inside, and proceeded to a minute[1515] inspection of the room. He darted from one object to the other with the[1516] agility of a grasshopper. I remained by the door, fearing to obliterate[1517] any clues. Poirot, however, did not seem grateful to me for my[1518] forbearance.[1519] [13]
[1520] “What have you, my friend,” he cried, “that you remain there like—how[1521] do you say it?—ah, yes, the stuck pig?”[1522] [13]
[1523] I explained that I was afraid of obliterating any foot-marks.[1524] [13]
[1525] “Foot-marks? But what an idea! There has already been practically an[1526] army in the room! What foot-marks are we likely to find? No, come here[1527] and aid me in my search. I will put down my little case until I need[1528] it.”[1529] [13]
[1530] He did so, on the round table by the window, but it was an ill-advised[1531] proceeding; for, the top of it being loose, it tilted up, and[1532] precipitated the despatch-case on the floor.[1533] [13]
[1534] “_Eh voilà une table!_” cried Poirot. “Ah, my friend, one may live in a[1535] big house and yet have no comfort.”[1536] [13]
[1537] After which piece of moralizing, he resumed his search.[1538] [13]
[1539] A small purple despatch-case, with a key in the lock, on the[1540] writing-table, engaged his attention for some time. He took out the key[1541] from the lock, and passed it to me to inspect. I saw nothing peculiar,[1542] however. It was an ordinary key of the Yale type, with a bit of twisted[1543] wire through the handle.[1544] [13]
[1545] Next, he examined the framework of the door we had broken in, assuring[1546] himself that the bolt had really been shot. Then he went to the door[1547] opposite leading into Cynthia’s room. That door was also bolted, as I[1548] had stated. However, he went to the length of unbolting it, and opening[1549] and shutting it several times; this he did with the utmost precaution[1550] against making any noise. Suddenly something in the bolt itself seemed[1551] to rivet his attention. He examined it carefully, and then, nimbly[1552] whipping out a pair of small forceps from his case, he drew out some[1553] minute particle which he carefully sealed up in a tiny envelope.[1554] [13]
[1555] On the chest of drawers there was a tray with a spirit lamp and a small[1556] saucepan on it. A small quantity of a dark fluid remained in the[1557] saucepan, and an empty cup and saucer that had been drunk out of stood[1558] near it.[1559] [13]
[1560] I wondered how I could have been so unobservant as to overlook this.[1561] Here was a clue worth having. Poirot delicately dipped his finger into[1562] liquid, and tasted it gingerly. He made a grimace.[1563] [13]
[1564] “Cocoa—with—I think—rum in it.”[1565] [13]
[1566] He passed on to the debris on the floor, where the table by the bed had[1567] been overturned. A reading-lamp, some books, matches, a bunch of keys,[1568] and the crushed fragments of a coffee-cup lay scattered about.[1569] [13]
[1570] “Ah, this is curious,” said Poirot.[1571] [13]
[1572] “I must confess that I see nothing particularly curious about it.”[1573] [13]
[1574] “You do not? Observe the lamp—the chimney is broken in two places; they[1575] lie there as they fell. But see, the coffee-cup is absolutely smashed[1576] to powder.”[1577] [13]
[1578] “Well,” I said wearily, “I suppose someone must have stepped on it.”[1579] [13]
[1580] “Exactly,” said Poirot, in an odd voice. “Someone stepped on it.”[1581] [13]
[1582] He rose from his knees, and walked slowly across to the mantelpiece,[1583] where he stood abstractedly fingering the ornaments, and straightening[1584] them—a trick of his when he was agitated.[1585] [13]
[1586] “_Mon ami_,” he said, turning to me, “somebody stepped on that cup,[1587] grinding it to powder, and the reason they did so was either because it[1588] contained strychnine or—which is far more serious—because it did not[1589] contain strychnine!”[1590] [13]
[1591] I made no reply. I was bewildered, but I knew that it was no good[1592] asking him to explain. In a moment or two he roused himself, and went[1593] on with his investigations. He picked up the bunch of keys from the[1594] floor, and twirling them round in his fingers finally selected one,[1595] very bright and shining, which he tried in the lock of the purple[1596] despatch-case. It fitted, and he opened the box, but after a moment’s[1597] hesitation, closed and relocked it, and slipped the bunch of keys, as[1598] well as the key that had originally stood in the lock, into his own[1599] pocket.[1600] [13]
[1601] “I have no authority to go through these papers. But it should be[1602] done—at once!”[1603] [13]
[1604] He then made a very careful examination of the drawers of the[1605] wash-stand. He went down on his knees, examining it minutely—even[1608] going so far as to smell it.[1609] [13]
[1610] Finally, he poured a few drops of the cocoa into a test tube, sealing[1611] it up carefully. His next proceeding was to take out a little notebook.[1612] [13]
[1613] “We have found in this room,” he said, writing busily, “six points of[1614] interest. Shall I enumerate them, or will you?”[1615] [13]
[1616] “Oh, you,” I replied hastily.[1617] [13]

[1622] “That may have been done some time ago,” I interrupted.[1623] [13]

One of my best hats once—but that is not[1636] to the point.”[1637] [13]
[1638] “It was very likely done last night. We were very agitated. Or perhaps[1639] Mrs. Inglethorp herself dropped her candle.”[1640] [13]
[1641] “You brought only one candle into the room?”[1642] [13]
[1643] “Yes. Lawrence Cavendish was carrying it. But he was very upset. He[1644] seemed to see something over here”—I indicated the mantelpiece—“that[1645] absolutely paralysed him.”[1646] [13]

[2107] “When you went into Mrs. Inglethorp’s room, was the door leading into[2108] Miss Cynthia’s room bolted?”[2109] [13]
