[3158] “Can you tell me what they were? Sulphonal? Veronal?”[3159] [13]
[3160] “No, they were bromide powders.”[3161] [13]
[3162] “Ah! Thank you, mademoiselle; good morning.”[3163] [13]
[3164] As we walked briskly away from the house, I glanced at him more than[3165] once. I had often before noticed that, if anything excited him, his[3166] eyes turned green like a cat’s. They were shining like emeralds now.[3167] [13]
[3168] “My friend,” he broke out at last, “I have a little idea, a very[3169] strange, and probably utterly impossible idea. And yet—it fits in.”[3170] [13]
[3171] I shrugged my shoulders. I privately thought that Poirot was rather too[3172] much given to these fantastic ideas. In this case, surely, the truth[3173] was only too plain and apparent.[3174] [13]
[3175] “So that is the explanation of the blank label on the box,” I remarked.[3176] “Very simple, as you said. I really wonder that I did not think of it[3177] myself.”[3178] [13]
[3179] Poirot did not appear to be listening to me.[3180] [13]
[3181] “They have made one more discovery, _là-bas_,” he observed, jerking his[3182] thumb over his shoulder in the direction of Styles. “Mr. Wells told me[3183] as we were going upstairs.”[3184] [13]
[3185] “What was it?”[3186] [13]
[3187] “Locked up in the desk in the boudoir, they found a will of Mrs.[3188] Inglethorp’s, dated before her marriage, leaving her fortune to Alfred[3189] Inglethorp. It must have been made just at the time they were engaged.[3190] It came quite as a surprise to Wells—and to John Cavendish also. It was[3191] written on one of those printed will forms, and witnessed by two of the[3192] servants—not Dorcas.”[3193] [13]
[3194] “Did Mr. Inglethorp know of it?”[3195] [13]
[3196] “He says not.”[3197] [13]
[3198] “One might take that with a grain of salt,” I remarked sceptically.[3199] “All these wills are very confusing. Tell me, how did those scribbled[3200] words on the envelope help you to discover that a will was made[3201] yesterday afternoon?”[3202] [13]
[3203] Poirot smiled.[3204] [13]
[3205] “_Mon ami_, have you ever, when writing a letter, been arrested by the[3206] fact that you did not know how to spell a certain word?”[3207] [13]
[3208] “Yes, often. I suppose everyone has.”[3209] [13]
[3210] “Exactly. And have you not, in such a case, tried the word once or[3211] twice on the edge of the blotting-paper, or a spare scrap of paper, to[3212] see if it looked right? Well, that is what Mrs. Inglethorp did. You[3213] will notice that the word ‘possessed’ is spelt first with one ‘s’ and[3214] subsequently with two—correctly. To make sure, she had further tried it[3215] in a sentence, thus: ‘I am possessed.’ Now, what did that tell me? It[3216] told me that Mrs. Inglethorp had been writing the word ‘possessed’ that[3217] afternoon, and, having the fragment of paper found in the grate fresh[3218] in my mind, the possibility of a will—(a document almost certain to[3219] contain that word)—occurred to me at once. This possibility was[3220] confirmed by a further circumstance. In the general confusion, the[3221] boudoir had not been swept that morning, and near the desk were several[3222] traces of brown mould and earth. The weather had been perfectly fine[3223] for some days, and no ordinary boots would have left such a heavy[3224] deposit.[3225] [13]
[3226] “I strolled to the window, and saw at once that the begonia beds had[3227] been newly planted. The mould in the beds was exactly similar to that[3228] on the floor of the boudoir, and also I learnt from you that they _had_[3229] been planted yesterday afternoon. I was now sure that one, or possibly[3230] both of the gardeners—for there were two sets of footprints in the[3231] bed—had entered the boudoir, for if Mrs. Inglethorp had merely wished[3232] to speak to them she would in all probability have stood at the window,[3233] and they would not have come into the room at all. I was now quite[3234] convinced that she had made a fresh will, and had called the two[3235] gardeners in to witness her signature. Events proved that I was right[3236] in my supposition.”[3237] [13]
[3238] “That was very ingenious,” I could not help admitting. “I must confess[3239] that the conclusions I drew from those few scribbled words were quite[3240] erroneous.”[3241] [13]
[3242] He smiled.[3243] [13]
[3244] “You gave too much rein to your imagination. Imagination is a good[3245] servant, and a bad master. The simplest explanation is always the most[3246] likely.”[3247] [13]
[3248] “Another point—how did you know that the key of the despatch-case had[3249] been lost?”[3250] [13]
[3251] “I did not know it. It was a guess that turned out to be correct. You[3252] observed that it had a piece of twisted wire through the handle. That[3253] suggested to me at once that it had possibly been wrenched off a flimsy[3254] key-ring. Now, if it had been lost and recovered, Mrs. Inglethorp would[3255] at once have replaced it on her bunch; but on her bunch I found what[3256] was obviously the duplicate key, very new and bright, which led me to[3257] the hypothesis that somebody else had inserted the original key in the[3258] lock of the despatch-case.”[3259] [13]
[3260] “Yes,” I said, “Alfred Inglethorp, without doubt.”[3261] [13]
[3262] Poirot looked at me curiously.[3263] [13]
[3264] “You are very sure of his guilt?”[3265] [13]
[3266] “Well, naturally. Every fresh circumstance seems to establish it more[3267] clearly.”[3268] [13]
[3269] “On the contrary,” said Poirot quietly, “there are several points in[3270] his favour.”[3271] [13]

[3301] I shook my head, unconvinced.[3302] [13]
[3303] “We do not agree, eh?” said Poirot. “Well, let us leave it. Time will[3304] show which of us is right. Now let us turn to other aspects of the[3305] case. What do you make of the fact that all the doors of the bedroom[3306] were bolted on the inside?”[3307] [13]

[3310] “True.”[3311] [13]

[3331] “But you agree with me that the door must have been opened by Mrs.[3332] Inglethorp herself?”[3333] [13]

[3364] We had reached Leastways Cottage, and Poirot ushered me upstairs to his[3365] own room. He offered me one of the tiny Russian cigarettes he himself[3366] occasionally smoked. I was amused to notice that he stowed away the[3367] used matches most carefully in a little china pot. My momentary[3368] annoyance vanished.[3369] [13]

[3400] “It’s all over the village about old Mrs. Inglethorp dying so suddenly.[3401] They do say—” he lowered his voice cautiously—“that it’s poison?”[3402] [13]
[3403] Poirot’s face remained quite impassive.[3404] [13]

[3487] [13]
[3488] [13]
[3489] [13]
[3490] CHAPTER VI.[3491] THE INQUEST[3492] [13]
[3493] [13]
[3494] In the interval before the inquest, Poirot was unfailing in his[3495] activity. Twice he was closeted with Mr. Wells. He also took long walks[3496] into the country. I rather resented his not taking me into his[3497] confidence, the more so as I could not in the least guess what he was[3498] driving at.[3499] [13]
[3500] It occurred to me that he might have been making inquiries at Raikes’s[3501] farm; so, finding him out when I called at Leastways Cottage on[3502] Wednesday evening, I walked over there by the fields, hoping to meet[3503] him. But there was no sign of him, and I hesitated to go right up to[3504] the farm itself. As I walked away, I met an aged rustic, who leered at[3505] me cunningly.[3506] [13]
[3507] “You’m from the Hall, bain’t you?” he asked.[3508] [13]
[3509] “Yes. I’m looking for a friend of mine whom I thought might have walked[3510] this way.”[3511] [13]
[3512] “A little chap? As waves his hands when he talks? One of them Belgies[3513] from the village?”[3514] [13]
[3515] “Yes,” I said eagerly. “He has been here, then?”[3516] [13]
[3517] “Oh, ay, he’s been here, right enough. More’n once too. Friend of[3518] yours, is he? Ah, you gentlemen from the Hall—you’m a pretty lot!” And[3519] he leered more jocosely than ever.[3520] [13]
[3521] “Why, do the gentlemen from the Hall come here often?” I asked, as[3522] carelessly as I could.[3523] [13]
[3524] He winked at me knowingly.[3525] [13]
[3526] “_One_ does, mister. Naming no names, mind. And a very liberal[3527] gentleman too! Oh, thank you, sir, I’m sure.”[3528] [13]
[3529] I walked on sharply. Evelyn Howard had been right then, and I[3530] experienced a sharp twinge of disgust, as I thought of Alfred[3531] Inglethorp’s liberality with another woman’s money. Had that piquant[3532] gipsy face been at the bottom of the crime, or was it the baser[3533] mainspring of money? Probably a judicious mixture of both.[3534] [13]
[3535] On one point, Poirot seemed to have a curious obsession. He once or[3536] twice observed to me that he thought Dorcas must have made an error in[3537] fixing the time of the quarrel. He suggested to her repeatedly that it[3538] was four-thirty, and not four o’clock when she had heard the voices.[3539] [13]
[3540] But Dorcas was unshaken. Quite an hour, or even more, had elapsed[3541] between the time when she had heard the voices and five o’clock, when[3542] she had taken tea to her mistress.[3543] [13]
[3544] The inquest was held on Friday at the Stylites Arms in the village.[3545] Poirot and I sat together, not being required to give evidence.[3546] [13]
[3547] The preliminaries were gone through. The jury viewed the body, and John[3548] Cavendish gave evidence of identification.[3549] [13]
[3550] Further questioned, he described his awakening in the early hours of[3551] the morning, and the circumstances of his mother’s death.[3552] [13]
[3553] The medical evidence was next taken. There was a breathless hush, and[3554] every eye was fixed on the famous London specialist, who was known to[3555] be one of the greatest authorities of the day on the subject of[3556] toxicology.[3557] [13]
[3558] In a few brief words, he summed up the result of the post-mortem. Shorn[3559] of its medical phraseology and technicalities, it amounted to the fact[3560] that Mrs. Inglethorp had met her death as the result of strychnine[3561] poisoning. Judging from the quantity recovered, she must have taken not[3562] less than three-quarters of a grain of strychnine, but probably one[3563] grain or slightly over.[3564] [13]
[3565] “Is it possible that she could have swallowed the poison by accident?”[3566] asked the Coroner.[3567] [13]
[3568] “I should consider it very unlikely. Strychnine is not used for[3569] domestic purposes, as some poisons are, and there are restrictions[3570] placed on its sale.”[3571] [13]
[3572] “Does anything in your examination lead you to determine how the poison[3573] was administered?”[3574] [13]
[3575] “No.”[3576] [13]
[3577] “You arrived at Styles before Dr. Wilkins, I believe?”[3578] [13]
[3579] “That is so. The motor met me just outside the lodge gates, and I[3580] hurried there as fast as I could.”[3581] [13]
[3582] “Will you relate to us exactly what happened next?”[3583] [13]
[3584] “I entered Mrs. Inglethorp’s room. She was at that moment in a typical[3585] tetanic convulsion. She turned towards me, and gasped out:[3586] ‘Alfred—Alfred——’”[3587] [13]
[3588] “Could the strychnine have been administered in Mrs. Inglethorp’s[3589] after-dinner coffee which was taken to her by her husband?”[3590] [13]
[3591] “Possibly, but strychnine is a fairly rapid drug in its action. The[3592] symptoms appear from one to two hours after it has been swallowed. It[3593] is retarded under certain conditions, none of which, however, appear to[3594] have been present in this case. I presume Mrs. Inglethorp took the[3595] coffee after dinner about eight o’clock, whereas the symptoms did not[3596] manifest themselves until the early hours of the morning, which, on the[3597] face of it, points to the drug having been taken much later in the[3598] evening.”[3599] [13]
[3600] “Mrs. Inglethorp was in the habit of drinking a cup of cocoa in the[3601] middle of the night. Could the strychnine have been administered in[3602] that?”[3603] [13]
[3604] “No, I myself took a sample of the cocoa remaining in the saucepan and[3605] had it analysed. There was no strychnine present.”[3606] [13]
[3607] I heard Poirot chuckle softly beside me.[3608] [13]
[3609] “How did you know?” I whispered.[3610] [13]
[3611] “Listen.”[3612] [13]
[3613] “I should say”—the doctor was continuing—“that I would have been[3614] considerably surprised at any other result.”[3615] [13]
[3616] “Why?”[3617] [13]
[3618] “Simply because strychnine has an unusually bitter taste. It can be[3619] detected in a solution of one in seventy thousand, and can only be[3620] disguised by some strongly flavoured substance. Cocoa would be quite[3621] powerless to mask it.”[3622] [13]
[3623] One of the jury wanted to know if the same objection applied to coffee.[3624] [13]
[3625] “No. Coffee has a bitter taste of its own which would probably cover[3626] the taste of strychnine.”[3627] [13]
[3628] “Then you consider it more likely that the drug was administered in the[3629] coffee, but that for some unknown reason its action was delayed.”[3630] [13]
[3631] “Yes, but, the cup being completely smashed, there is no possibility of[3632] analyzing its contents.”[3633] [13]
[3634] This concluded Dr. Bauerstein’s evidence. Dr. Wilkins corroborated it[3635] on all points. Sounded as to the possibility of suicide, he repudiated[3636] it utterly. The deceased, he said, suffered from a weak heart, but[3637] otherwise enjoyed perfect health, and was of a cheerful and[3638] well-balanced disposition. She would be one of the last people to take[3639] her own life.[3640] [13]
[3641] Lawrence Cavendish was next called. His evidence was quite unimportant,[3642] being a mere repetition of that of his brother. Just as he was about to[3643] step down, he paused, and said rather hesitatingly:[3644] [13]
[3645] “I should like to make a suggestion if I may?”[3646] [13]
[3647] He glanced deprecatingly at the Coroner, who replied briskly:[3648] [13]
[3649] “Certainly, Mr. Cavendish, we are here to arrive at the truth of this[3650] matter, and welcome anything that may lead to further elucidation.”[3651] [13]
[3652] “It is just an idea of mine,” explained Lawrence. “Of course I may be[3653] quite wrong, but it still seems to me that my mother’s death might be[3654] accounted for by natural means.”[3655] [13]
[3656] “How do you make that out, Mr. Cavendish?”[3657] [13]
[3658] “My mother, at the time of her death, and for some time before it, was[3659] taking a tonic containing strychnine.”[3660] [13]
[3661] “Ah!” said the Coroner.[3662] [13]
[3663] The jury looked up, interested.[3664] [13]
[3665] “I believe,” continued Lawrence, “that there have been cases where the[3666] cumulative effect of a drug, administered for some time, has ended by[3667] causing death. Also, is it not possible that she may have taken an[3668] overdose of her medicine by accident?”[3669] [13]
[3670] “This is the first we have heard of the deceased taking strychnine at[3671] the time of her death. We are much obliged to you, Mr. Cavendish.”[3672] [13]
[3673] Dr. Wilkins was recalled and ridiculed the idea.[3674] [13]
[3675] “What Mr. Cavendish suggests is quite impossible. Any doctor would tell[3676] you the same. Strychnine is, in a certain sense, a cumulative poison,[3677] but it would be quite impossible for it to result in sudden death in[3678] this way. There would have to be a long period of chronic symptoms[3679] which would at once have attracted my attention. The whole thing is[3680] absurd.”[3681] [13]
[3682] “And the second suggestion? That Mrs. Inglethorp may have inadvertently[3683] taken an overdose?”[3684] [13]
[3685] “Three, or even four doses, would not have resulted in death. Mrs.[3686] Inglethorp always had an extra large amount of medicine made up at a[3687] time, as she dealt with Coot’s, the Cash Chemists in Tadminster. She[3688] would have had to take very nearly the whole bottle to account for the[3689] amount of strychnine found at the post-mortem.”[3690] [13]
[3691] “Then you consider that we may dismiss the tonic as not being in any[3692] way instrumental in causing her death?”[3693] [13]
[3694] “Certainly. The supposition is ridiculous.”[3695] [13]
[3696] The same juryman who had interrupted before here suggested that the[3697] chemist who made up the medicine might have committed an error.[3698] [13]
[3699] “That, of course, is always possible,” replied the doctor.[3700] [13]
[3701] But Dorcas, who was the next witness called, dispelled even that[3702] possibility. The medicine had not been newly made up. On the contrary,[3703] Mrs. Inglethorp had taken the last dose on the day of her death.[3704] [13]
[3705] So the question of the tonic was finally abandoned, and the Coroner[3706] proceeded with his task. Having elicited from Dorcas how she had been[3707] awakened by the violent ringing of her mistress’s bell, and had[3708] subsequently roused the household, he passed to the subject of the[3709] quarrel on the preceding afternoon.[3710] [13]
[3711] Dorcas’s evidence on this point was substantially what Poirot and I had[3712] already heard, so I will not repeat it here.[3713] [13]
[3714] The next witness was Mary Cavendish. She stood very upright, and spoke[3715] in a low, clear, and perfectly composed voice. In answer to the[3716] Coroner’s question, she told how, her alarm clock having aroused her at[3717] four-thirty as usual, she was dressing, when she was startled by the[3718] sound of something heavy falling.[3719] [13]
[3720] “That would have been the table by the bed?” commented the Coroner.[3721] [13]
[3722] “I opened my door,” continued Mary, “and listened. In a few minutes a[3723] bell rang violently. Dorcas came running down and woke my husband, and[3724] we all went to my mother-in-law’s room, but it was locked——”[3725] [13]
[3726] The Coroner interrupted her.[3727] [13]
[3728] “I really do not think we need trouble you further on that point. We[3729] know all that can be known of the subsequent happenings. But I should[3730] be obliged if you would tell us all you overheard of the quarrel the[3731] day before.”[3732] [13]
[3733] “I?”[3734] [13]
[3735] There was a faint insolence in her voice. She raised her hand and[3736] adjusted the ruffle of lace at her neck, turning her head a little as[3737] she did so. And quite spontaneously the thought flashed across my mind:[3738] “She is gaining time!”[3739] [13]

[3744] This was news to me and glancing sideways at Poirot, I fancied that it[3745] was news to him as well.[3746] [13]
[3747] There was the faintest pause, the mere hesitation of a moment, before[3748] she answered:[3749] [13]
[3750] “Yes, that is so.”[3751] [13]
[3752] “And the boudoir window was open, was it not?”[3753] [13]
[3754] Surely her face grew a little paler as she answered:[3755] [13]
[3756] “Yes.”[3757] [13]
[3758] “Then you cannot have failed to hear the voices inside, especially as[3759] they were raised in anger. In fact, they would be more audible where[3760] you were than in the hall.”[3761] [13]
[3762] “Possibly.”[3763] [13]
[3764] “Will you repeat to us what you overheard of the quarrel?”[3765] [13]
[3766] “I really do not remember hearing anything.”[3767] [13]

[3774] The Coroner persisted.[3775] [13]

[3799] “That is all.”[3800] [13]

[3805] Amy Hill, shop assistant, was next called, and deposed to having sold a[3806] will form on the afternoon of the 17th to William Earl, under-gardener[3807] at Styles.[3808] [13]
[3809] William Earl and Manning succeeded her, and testified to witnessing a[3810] document. Manning fixed the time at about four-thirty, William was of[3811] the opinion that it was rather earlier.[3812] [13]

[3825] “Miss Howard.”[3826] [13]
[3827] Miss Howard produced the letter written to her by Mrs. Inglethorp on[3828] the evening of the 17th. Poirot and I had, of course already seen it.[3829] It added nothing to our knowledge of the tragedy. The following is a[3830] facsimile:[3831] [13]
[3832] STYLES COURT[3833] ESSEX[3834] hand written note:[3835] [13]
[3836]      July 17th[3837]      My dear Evelyn[3838]      Can we not bury[3839]      the hachet? [3878] Then came the sensation of the day. The Coroner called Albert Mace,[3879] chemist’s assistant.[3880] [13]

[3888] “Mr. Mace, have you lately sold strychnine to any unauthorized person?”[3889] [13]

[4103] CHAPTER VII.[4104] POIROT PAYS HIS DEBTS[4105] [13]
[4106] [13]
[4107] As we came out of the Stylites Arms, Poirot drew me aside by a gentle[4108] pressure of the arm. I understood his object. He was waiting for the[4109] Scotland Yard men.[4110] [13]
[4111] In a few moments, they emerged, and Poirot at once stepped forward, and[4112] accosted the shorter of the two.[4113] [13]

It was in 1904 he and[4118] I worked together—the Abercrombie forgery case—you remember, he was run[4119] down in Brussels. Ah, those were great days, moosier. Then, do you[4120] remember ‘Baron’ Altara? There was a pretty rogue for you! He eluded[4121] the clutches of half the police in Europe. But we nailed him in[4122] Antwerp—thanks to Mr. Poirot here.”[4123] [13]

[4128] “I need hardly ask what you are doing here, gentlemen,” remarked[4129] Poirot.[4130] [13]
[4131] Japp closed one eye knowingly.[4132] [13]
[4133] “No, indeed. Pretty clear case I should say.”[4134] [13]
[4135] But Poirot answered gravely:[4136] [13]
[4137] “There I differ from you.”[4138] [13]

[4152] “I have drawn certain conclusions—yes.”[4153] [13]
[4154] Summerhaye was still looking rather sceptical, but Japp continued his[4155] scrutiny of Poirot.[4156] [13]
[4157] “It’s this way,” he said, “so far, we’ve only seen the case from the[4158] outside. That’s where the Yard’s at a disadvantage in a case of this[4159] kind, where the murder’s only out, so to speak, after the inquest. A[4160] lot depends on being on the spot first thing, and that’s where Mr.[4161] Poirot’s had the start of us. We shouldn’t have been here as soon as[4162] this even, if it hadn’t been for the fact that there was a smart doctor[4163] on the spot, who gave us the tip through the Coroner. From the evidence at the inquest, Mr. Inglethorp murdered his[4166] wife as sure as I stand here, and if anyone but you hinted the contrary[4167] I’d laugh in his face. [4171] “Perhaps, though, you have a warrant for his arrest in your pocket[4172] now,” suggested Poirot.[4173] [13]

At Styles, Mr. Inglethorp will give you, or if he refuses—as[4223] is probable—I will give you such proofs that shall satisfy you that the[4224] case against him could not possibly be sustained. Is that a bargain?”[4225] [13]
[4226] “That’s a bargain,” said Japp heartily. “And, on behalf of the Yard,[4227] I’m much obliged to you, though I’m bound to confess I can’t at present[4228] see the faintest possible loop-hole in the evidence, but you always[4229] were a marvel! So long, then, moosier.”[4230] [13]
[4231] The two detectives strode away, Summerhaye with an incredulous grin on[4232] his face.[4233] [13]
[4234] “Well, my friend,” cried Poirot, before I could get in a word, “what do[4235] you think? _Mon Dieu!_ I had some warm moments in that court; I did not[4236] figure to myself that the man would be so pig-headed as to refuse to[4237] say anything at all. Decidedly, it was the policy of an imbecile.”[4238] [13]
[4239] “H’m! There are other explanations besides that of imbecility,” I[4240] remarked. “For, if the case against him is true, how could he defend[4241] himself except by silence?”[4242] [13]
[4243] “Why, in a thousand ingenious ways,” cried Poirot. “See; say that it is[4244] I who have committed this murder, I can think of seven most plausible[4245] stories! Far more convincing than Mr. Inglethorp’s stony denials!”[4246] [13]
[4247] I could not help laughing.[4248] [13]
[4249] “My dear Poirot, I am sure you are capable of thinking of seventy! But,[4250] seriously, in spite of what I heard you say to the detectives, you[4251] surely cannot still believe in the possibility of Alfred Inglethorp’s[4252] innocence?”[4253] [13]
[4254] “Why not now as much as before? Nothing has changed.”[4255] [13]
[4256] “But the evidence is so conclusive.”[4257] [13]
[4258] “Yes, too conclusive.”[4259] [13]
[4260] We turned in at the gate of Leastways Cottage, and proceeded up the now[4261] familiar stairs.[4262] [13]
[4263] “Yes, yes, too conclusive,” continued Poirot, almost to himself. “Real[4264] evidence is usually vague and unsatisfactory. It has to be[4265] examined—sifted. [4269] “How do you make that out?”[4270] [13]
[4271] “Because, so long as the evidence against him was vague and intangible,[4272] it was very hard to disprove. But, in his anxiety, the criminal has[4273] drawn the net so closely that one cut will set Inglethorp free.”[4274] [13]
[4275] I was silent. And in a minute or two, Poirot continued:[4276] [13]
[4277] “Let us look at the matter like this. Here is a man, let us say, who[4278] sets out to poison his wife. He has lived by his wits as the saying[4279] goes. Presumably, therefore, he has some wits. He is not altogether a[4280] fool. Well, how does he set about it? He goes boldly to the village[4281] chemist’s and purchases strychnine under his own name, with a trumped[4282] up story about a dog which is bound to be proved absurd. He does not[4283] employ the poison that night. No, he waits until he has had a violent[4284] quarrel with her, of which the whole household is cognisant, and which[4285] naturally directs their suspicions upon him. He prepares no defence—no[4286] shadow of an alibi, yet he knows the chemist’s assistant must[4287] necessarily come forward with the facts. Bah! [4291] “Still—I do not see——” I began.[4292] [13]
[4293] “Neither do I see. I tell you, _mon ami_, it puzzles me. _Me_—Hercule[4294] Poirot!”[4295] [13]
[4296] “But if you believe him innocent, how do you explain his buying the[4297] strychnine?”[4298] [13]
[4299] “Very simply. He did _not_ buy it.”[4300] [13]
[4301] “But Mace recognized him!”[4302] [13]
[4303] “I beg your pardon, he saw a man with a black beard like Mr.[4304] Inglethorp’s, and wearing glasses like Mr. Inglethorp, and dressed in[4305] Mr. Inglethorp’s rather noticeable clothes. [4498] I think the appearance of the two Scotland Yard men was rather a[4499] shock—especially to John, though of course after the verdict, he had[4500] realized that it was only a matter of time. Still, the presence of the[4501] detectives brought the truth home to him more than anything else could[4502] have done.[4503] [13]

[4990] The papers, of course, had been full of the tragedy. [5351] [13]
[5352] I had had no opportunity as yet of passing on Poirot’s message to[5353] Lawrence. [5550] The woods round Styles were very beautiful. [5764] “H’m, yes, that might be,” said John. “But look here, how could he have[5765] got at the cocoa? That wasn’t downstairs?”[5766] [13]
[5767] “No, it wasn’t,” I admitted reluctantly.[5768] [13]
[5769] And then, suddenly, a dreadful possibility flashed through my mind. I[5770] hoped and prayed it would not occur to John also. I glanced sideways at[5771] him. He was frowning perplexedly, and I drew a deep breath of relief,[5772] for the terrible thought that had flashed across my mind was this: that[5773] Dr. Bauerstein might have had an accomplice.[5774] [13]
[5775] Yet surely it could not be! Surely no woman as beautiful as Mary[5776] Cavendish could be a murderess. Yet beautiful women had been known to[5777] poison.[5778] [13]
[5779] And suddenly I remembered that first conversation at tea on the day of[5780] my arrival, and the gleam in her eyes as she had said that poison was a[5781] woman’s weapon. How agitated she had been on that fatal Tuesday[5782] evening! Had Mrs. Inglethorp discovered something between her and[5783] Bauerstein, and threatened to tell her husband? Was it to stop that[5784] denunciation that the crime had been committed?[5785] [13]
[5786] Then I remembered that enigmatical conversation between Poirot and[5787] Evelyn Howard. Was this what they had meant? Was this the monstrous[5788] possibility that Evelyn had tried not to believe?[5789] [13]
[5790] Yes, it all fitted in.[5791] [13]
[5792] No wonder Miss Howard had suggested “hushing it up.” Now I understood[5793] that unfinished sentence of hers: “Emily herself——” And in my heart I[5794] agreed with her. Would not Mrs. Inglethorp have preferred to go[5795] unavenged rather than have such terrible dishonour fall upon the name[5796] of Cavendish.[5797] [13]
[5798] “There’s another thing,” said John suddenly, and the unexpected sound[5799] of his voice made me start guiltily. “Something which makes me doubt if[5800] what you say can be true.”[5801] [13]

[5805] “Why, the fact that Bauerstein demanded a post-mortem. He needn’t have[5806] done so. Little Wilkins would have been quite content to let it go at[5807] heart disease.”[5808] [13]
[5809] “Yes,” I said doubtfully. “But we don’t know. Perhaps he thought it[5810] safer in the long run. Someone might have talked afterwards. [5819] I trembled.[5820] [13]
[5821] “Look here,” I said, “I may be altogether wrong. And, remember, all[5822] this is in confidence.”[5823] [13]
[5824] “Oh, of course—that goes without saying.”[5825] [13]

He made me take the brooch out of my tie the other day, and[5836] put it in again, because he said it wasn’t straight.”[5837] [13]

[5840] “It’s quite a mania with him.”[5841] [13]
[5842] “Yes, isn’t it?”[5843] [13]

[6005] [13]

[6007] [13]
[6008] CHAPTER X.[6009] THE ARREST[6010] [13]
[6011] [13]
[6012] To my extreme annoyance, Poirot was not in, and the old Belgian who[6013] answered my knock informed me that he believed he had gone to London.[6014] [13]
[6015] I was dumbfounded. What on earth could Poirot be doing in London! Was[6016] it a sudden decision on his part, or had he already made up his mind[6017] when he parted from me a few hours earlier?[6018] [13]
[6019] I retraced my steps to Styles in some annoyance. With Poirot away, I[6020] was uncertain how to act. Had he foreseen this arrest? Had he not, in[6021] all probability, been the cause of it? Those questions I could not[6022] resolve. But in the meantime what was I to do? Should I announce the[6023] arrest openly at Styles, or not? Though I did not acknowledge it to[6024] myself, the thought of Mary Cavendish was weighing on me. Would it not[6025] be a terrible shock to her? For the moment, I set aside utterly any[6026] suspicions of her. She could not be implicated—otherwise I should have[6027] heard some hint of it.[6028] [13]
[6029] Of course, there was no possibility of being able permanently to[6030] conceal Dr. Bauerstein’s arrest from her. It would be announced in[6031] every newspaper on the morrow. Still, I shrank from blurting it out. If[6032] only Poirot had been accessible, I could have asked his advice. What[6033] possessed him to go posting off to London in this unaccountable way?[6034] [13]
[6035] In spite of myself, my opinion of his sagacity was immeasurably[6036] heightened. I would never have dreamt of suspecting the doctor, had not[6037] Poirot put it into my head. Yes, decidedly, the little man was clever.[6038] [13]
[6039] After some reflecting, I decided to take John into my confidence, and[6040] leave him to make the matter public or not, as he thought fit.[6041] [13]
[6042] He gave vent to a prodigious whistle, as I imparted the news.[6043] [13]
[6044] “Great Scott! You _were_ right, then. I couldn’t believe it at the[6045] time.”[6046] [13]
[6047] “No, it is astonishing until you get used to the idea, and see how it[6048] makes everything fit in. Now, what are we to do? Of course, it will be[6049] generally known to-morrow.”[6050] [13]
[6051] John reflected.[6052] [13]
[6053] “Never mind,” he said at last, “we won’t say anything at present. There[6054] is no need. As you say, it will be known soon enough.”[6055] [13]
[6056] But to my intense surprise, on getting down early the next morning, and[6057] eagerly opening the newspapers, there was not a word about the arrest![6058] There was a column of mere padding about “The Styles Poisoning Case,”[6059] but nothing further. It was rather inexplicable, but I supposed that,[6060] for some reason or other, Japp wished to keep it out of the papers. It[6061] worried me just a little, for it suggested the possibility that there[6062] might be further arrests to come.[6063] [13]
[6064] After breakfast, I decided to go down to the village, and see if Poirot[6065] had returned yet; but, before I could start, a well-known face blocked[6066] one of the windows, and the well-known voice said:[6067] [13]
[6068] “_Bonjour, mon ami!_”[6069] [13]

[6081] “Did you not know it?”[6082] [13]

[6285] “I see.”[6286] [13]
[6287] In spite of her laugh, Mary was looking thoughtful this morning. She[6288] seemed grave, almost sad.[6289] [13]
[6290] It occurred to me that it would be a good opportunity to tackle her on[6291] the subject of Cynthia. I began rather tactfully, _I_ thought, but I[6292] had not gone far before she stopped me authoritatively.[6293] [13]
[6294] “You are an excellent advocate, I have no doubt, Mr. Hastings, but in[6295] this case your talents are quite thrown away. Cynthia will run no risk[6296] of encountering any unkindness from me.”[6297] [13]

[6308] “Well,” she said quietly, “whether it is your business or not, I will[6309] tell you that we are _not_ happy.”[6310] [13]
[6311] I said nothing, for I saw that she had not finished.[6312] [13]
[6313] She began slowly, walking up and down the room, her head a little bent,[6314] and that slim, supple figure of hers swaying gently as she walked. She[6315] stopped suddenly, and looked up at me.[6316] [13]

[6334] “A hint of something foreign—different—that there has always been about[6335] you.”[6336] [13]
[6337] “My mother was very beautiful, I believe. I don’t know, because I never[6338] saw her. She died when I was quite a little child. I believe there was[6339] some tragedy connected with her death—she took an overdose of some[6340] sleeping draught by mistake. However that may be, my father was[6341] broken-hearted. Shortly afterwards, he went into the Consular Service.[6342] Everywhere he went, I went with him. When I was twenty-three, I had[6343] been nearly all over the world. [6345] There was a smile on her face, and her head was thrown back. She seemed[6346] living in the memory of those old glad days.[6347] [13]
[6348] “Then my father died. He left me very badly off. [6355] “Yes?”[6356] [13]

[6439] No, surely she could not care for Bauerstein. No woman could act her[6440] part with that icy unconcern.[6441] [13]

[6744] CHAPTER XI.[6745] THE CASE FOR THE PROSECUTION[6746] [13]
[6747] [13]
[6748] The trial of John Cavendish for the murder of his stepmother took place[6749] two months later.[6750] [13]
[6751] Of the intervening weeks I will say little, but my admiration and[6752] sympathy went out unfeignedly to Mary Cavendish. She ranged herself[6753] passionately on her husband’s side, scorning the mere idea of his[6754] guilt, and fought for him tooth and nail.[6755] [13]
[6756] I expressed my admiration to Poirot, and he nodded thoughtfully.[6757] [13]
[6758] “Yes, she is of those women who show at their best in adversity. It[6759] brings out all that is sweetest and truest in them. Her pride and her[6760] jealousy have——”[6761] [13]
[6762] “Jealousy?” I queried.[6763] [13]
[6764] “Yes. Have you not realized that she is an unusually jealous woman? As[6765] I was saying, her pride and jealousy have been laid aside. She thinks[6766] of nothing but her husband, and the terrible fate that is hanging over[6767] him.”[6768] [13]
[6769] He spoke very feelingly, and I looked at him earnestly, remembering[6770] that last afternoon, when he had been deliberating whether or not to[6771] speak. With his tenderness for “a woman’s happiness,” I felt glad that[6772] the decision had been taken out of his hands.[6773] [13]

[6811] “When did you first suspect John Cavendish?” I asked, after a minute or[6812] two.[6813] [13]
[6814] “Did you not suspect him at all?”[6815] [13]
[6816] “No, indeed.”[6817] [13]
[6818] “Not after that fragment of conversation you overheard between Mrs.[6819] Cavendish and her mother-in-law, and her subsequent lack of frankness[6820] at the inquest?”[6821] [13]
[6822] “No.”[6823] [13]
[6824] “Did you not put two and two together, and reflect that if it was not[6825] Alfred Inglethorp who was quarrelling with his wife—and you remember,[6826] he strenuously denied it at the inquest—it must be either Lawrence or[6827] John. Now, if it was Lawrence, Mary Cavendish’s conduct was just as[6828] inexplicable. But if, on the other hand, it was John, the whole thing[6829] was explained quite naturally.”[6830] [13]
[6831] “So,” I cried, a light breaking in upon me, “it was John who quarrelled[6832] with his mother that afternoon?”[6833] [13]
[6834] “Exactly.”[6835] [13]
[6836] “And you have known this all along?”[6837] [13]
[6838] “Certainly. Mrs. Cavendish’s behaviour could only be explained that[6839] way.”[6840] [13]
[6841] “And yet you say he may be acquitted?”[6842] [13]
[6843] Poirot shrugged his shoulders.[6844] [13]
[6845] “Certainly I do. At the police court proceedings, we shall hear the[6846] case for the prosecution, but in all probability his solicitors will[6847] advise him to reserve his defence. That will be sprung upon us at the[6848] trial. And—ah, by the way, I have a word of caution to give you, my[6849] friend. I must not appear in the case.”[6850] [13]
[6851] “What?”[6852] [13]
[6853] “No. Officially, I have nothing to do with it. Until I have found that[6854] last link in my chain, I must remain behind the scenes. Mrs. Cavendish[6855] must think I am working for her husband, not against him.”[6856] [13]
[6857] “I say, that’s playing it a bit low down,” I protested.[6858] [13]
[6859] “Not at all. We have to deal with a most clever and unscrupulous man,[6860] and we must use any means in our power—otherwise he will slip through[6861] our fingers. That is why I have been careful to remain in the[6862] background. All the discoveries have been made by Japp, and Japp will[6863] take all the credit. If I am called upon to give evidence at all”—he[6864] smiled broadly—“it will probably be as a witness for the defence.”[6865] [13]
[6866] I could hardly believe my ears.[6867] [13]
[6868] “It is quite _en règle_,” continued Poirot. “Strangely enough, I can[6869] give evidence that will demolish one contention of the prosecution.”[6870] [13]
[6871] “Which one?”[6872] [13]
[6873] “The one that relates to the destruction of the will. John Cavendish[6874] did not destroy that will.”[6875] [13]
[6876] Poirot was a true prophet. I will not go into the details of the police[6877] court proceedings, as it involves many tiresome repetitions. I will[6878] merely state baldly that John Cavendish reserved his defence, and was[6879] duly committed for trial.[6880] [13]
[6881] September found us all in London. Mary took a house in Kensington,[6882] Poirot being included in the family party.[6883] [13]
[6884] I myself had been given a job at the War Office, so was able to see[6885] them continually.[6886] [13]
[6887] As the weeks went by, the state of Poirot’s nerves grew worse and[6888] worse. That “last link” he talked about was still lacking. Privately, I[6889] hoped it might remain so, for what happiness could there be for Mary,[6890] if John were not acquitted?[6891] [13]
[6892] On September 15th John Cavendish appeared in the dock at the Old[6893] Bailey, charged with “The Wilful Murder of Emily Agnes Inglethorp,” and[6894] pleaded “Not Guilty.”[6895] [13]
[6896] Sir Ernest Heavywether, the famous K.C., had been engaged to defend[6897] him.[6898] [13]
[6899] Mr. Philips, K.C., opened the case for the Crown.[6900] [13]
[6901] The murder, he said, was a most premeditated and cold-blooded one. It[6902] was neither more nor less than the deliberate poisoning of a fond and[6903] trusting woman by the stepson to whom she had been more than a mother.[6904] Ever since his boyhood, she had supported him. He and his wife had[6905] lived at Styles Court in every luxury, surrounded by her care and[6906] attention. She had been their kind and generous benefactress.[6907] [13]
[6908] He proposed to call witnesses to show how the prisoner, a profligate[6909] and spendthrift, had been at the end of his financial tether, and had[6910] also been carrying on an intrigue with a certain Mrs. Raikes, a[6911] neighbouring farmer’s wife. This having come to his stepmother’s ears,[6912] she taxed him with it on the afternoon before her death, and a quarrel[6913] ensued, part of which was overheard. On the previous day, the prisoner[6914] had purchased strychnine at the village chemist’s shop, wearing a[6915] disguise by means of which he hoped to throw the onus of the crime upon[6916] another man—to wit, Mrs. Inglethorp’s husband, of whom he had been[6917] bitterly jealous. Luckily for Mr. Inglethorp, he had been able to[6918] produce an unimpeachable alibi.[6919] [13]
[6920] On the afternoon of July 17th, continued Counsel, immediately after the[6921] quarrel with her son, Mrs. Inglethorp made a new will. This will was[6922] found destroyed in the grate of her bedroom the following morning, but[6923] evidence had come to light which showed that it had been drawn up in[6924] favour of her husband. Deceased had already made a will in his favour[6925] before her marriage, but—and Mr. Philips wagged an expressive[6926] forefinger—the prisoner was not aware of that. What had induced the[6927] deceased to make a fresh will, with the old one still extant, he could[6928] not say. She was an old lady, and might possibly have forgotten the[6929] former one; or—this seemed to him more likely—she may have had an idea[6930] that it was revoked by her marriage, as there had been some[6931] conversation on the subject. Ladies were not always very well versed in[6932] legal knowledge. She had, about a year before, executed a will in[6933] favour of the prisoner. He would call evidence to show that it was the[6934] prisoner who ultimately handed his stepmother her coffee on the fatal[6935] night. Later in the evening, he had sought admission to her room, on[6936] which occasion, no doubt, he found an opportunity of destroying the[6937] will which, as far as he knew, would render the one in his favour[6938] valid.[6939] [13]
[6940] The prisoner had been arrested in consequence of the discovery, in his[6941] room, by Detective Inspector Japp—a most brilliant officer—of the[6942] identical phial of strychnine which had been sold at the village[6943] chemist’s to the supposed Mr. Inglethorp on the day before the murder.[6944] It would be for the jury to decide whether or not these damning facts[6945] constituted an overwhelming proof of the prisoner’s guilt.[6946] [13]
[6947] And, subtly implying that a jury which did not so decide, was quite[6948] unthinkable, Mr. Philips sat down and wiped his forehead.[6949] [13]
[6950] The first witnesses for the prosecution were mostly those who had been[6951] called at the inquest, the medical evidence being again taken first.[6952] [13]
[6953] Sir Ernest Heavywether, who was famous all over England for the[6954] unscrupulous manner in which he bullied witnesses, only asked two[6955] questions.[6956] [13]
[6957] “I take it, Dr. Bauerstein, that strychnine, as a drug, acts quickly?”[6958] [13]
[6959] “Yes.”[6960] [13]
[6961] “And that you are unable to account for the delay in this case?”[6962] [13]
[6963] “Yes.”[6964] [13]
[6965] “Thank you.”[6966] [13]
[6967] Mr. Mace identified the phial handed him by Counsel as that sold by him[6968] to “Mr. Inglethorp.” Pressed, he admitted that he only knew Mr.[6969] Inglethorp by sight. He had never spoken to him. The witness was not[6970] cross-examined.[6971] [13]
[6972] Alfred Inglethorp was called, and denied having purchased the poison.[6973] He also denied having quarrelled with his wife. Various witnesses[6974] testified to the accuracy of these statements.[6975] [13]
[6976] The gardeners’ evidence, as to the witnessing of the will was taken,[6977] and then Dorcas was called.[6978] [13]
[6979] Dorcas, faithful to her “young gentlemen,” denied strenuously that it[6980] could have been John’s voice she heard, and resolutely declared, in the[6981] teeth of everything, that it was Mr. Inglethorp who had been in the[6982] boudoir with her mistress. A rather wistful smile passed across the[6983] face of the prisoner in the dock. He knew only too well how useless her[6984] gallant defiance was, since it was not the object of the defence to[6985] deny this point. Mrs. Cavendish, of course, could not be called upon to[6986] give evidence against her husband.[6987] [13]
[6988] After various questions on other matters, Mr. Philips asked:[6989] [13]
[6990] “In the month of June last, do you remember a parcel arriving for Mr.[6991] Lawrence Cavendish from Parkson’s?”[6992] [13]
[6993] Dorcas shook her head.[6994] [13]
[6995] “I don’t remember, sir. It may have done, but Mr. Lawrence was away[6996] from home part of June.”[6997] [13]
[6998] “In the event of a parcel arriving for him whilst he was away, what[6999] would be done with it?”[7000] [13]
[7001] “It would either be put in his room or sent on after him.”[7002] [13]
[7003] “By you?”[7004] [13]
[7005] “No, sir, I should leave it on the hall table. It would be Miss Howard[7006] who would attend to anything like that.”[7007] [13]
[7008] Evelyn Howard was called and, after being examined on other points, was[7009] questioned as to the parcel.[7010] [13]
[7011] “Don’t remember. Lots of parcels come. Can’t remember one special one.”[7012] [13]
[7013] “You do not know if it was sent after Mr. Lawrence Cavendish to Wales,[7014] or whether it was put in his room?”[7015] [13]
[7016] “Don’t think it was sent after him. Should have remembered it if it[7017] was.”[7018] [13]
[7019] “Supposing a parcel arrived addressed to Mr. Lawrence Cavendish, and[7020] afterwards it disappeared, should you remark its absence?”[7021] [13]
[7022] “No, don’t think so. I should think someone had taken charge of it.”[7023] [13]
[7024] “I believe, Miss Howard, that it was you who found this sheet of brown[7025] paper?” He held up the same dusty piece which Poirot and I had examined[7026] in the morning-room at Styles.[7027] [13]
[7028] “Yes, I did.”[7029] [13]
[7030] “How did you come to look for it?”[7031] [13]
[7032] “The Belgian detective who was employed on the case asked me to search[7033] for it.”[7034] [13]
[7035] “Where did you eventually discover it?”[7036] [13]
[7037] “On the top of—of—a wardrobe.”[7038] [13]
[7039] “On top of the prisoner’s wardrobe?”[7040] [13]
[7041] “I—I believe so.”[7042] [13]
[7043] “Did you not find it yourself?”[7044] [13]
[7045] “Yes.”[7046] [13]
[7047] “Then you must know where you found it?”[7048] [13]
[7049] “Yes, it was on the prisoner’s wardrobe.”[7050] [13]
[7051] “That is better.”[7052] [13]
[7053] An assistant from Parkson’s, Theatrical Costumiers, testified that on[7054] June 29th, they had supplied a black beard to Mr. L. Cavendish, as[7055] requested. It was ordered by letter, and a postal order was enclosed.[7056] No, they had not kept the letter. All transactions were entered in[7057] their books. They had sent the beard, as directed, to “L. Cavendish,[7058] Esq., Styles Court.”[7059] [13]
[7060] Sir Ernest Heavywether rose ponderously.[7061] [13]
[7062] “Where was the letter written from?”[7063] [13]
[7064] “From Styles Court.”[7065] [13]
[7066] “The same address to which you sent the parcel?”[7067] [13]
[7068] “Yes.”[7069] [13]
[7070] “And the letter came from there?”[7071] [13]
[7072] “Yes.”[7073] [13]
[7074] Like a beast of prey, Heavywether fell upon him:[7075] [13]
[7076] “How do you know?”[7077] [13]
[7078] “I—I don’t understand.”[7079] [13]
[7080] “How do you know that letter came from Styles? Did you notice the[7081] postmark?”[7082] [13]
[7083] “No—but——”[7084] [13]
[7085] “Ah, you did _not_ notice the postmark! And yet you affirm so[7086] confidently that it came from Styles. It might, in fact, have been any[7087] postmark?”[7088] [13]
[7089] “Y—es.”[7090] [13]
[7091] “In fact, the letter, though written on stamped notepaper, might have[7092] been posted from anywhere? From Wales, for instance?”[7093] [13]
[7094] The witness admitted that such might be the case, and Sir Ernest[7095] signified that he was satisfied.[7096] [13]
[7097] Elizabeth Wells, second housemaid at Styles, stated that after she had[7098] gone to bed she remembered that she had bolted the front door, instead[7099] of leaving it on the latch as Mr. Inglethorp had requested. She had[7100] accordingly gone downstairs again to rectify her error. Hearing a[7101] slight noise in the West wing, she had peeped along the passage, and[7102] had seen Mr. John Cavendish knocking at Mrs. Inglethorp’s door.[7103] [13]
[7104] Sir Ernest Heavywether made short work of her, and under his unmerciful[7105] bullying she contradicted herself hopelessly, and Sir Ernest sat down[7106] again with a satisfied smile on his face.[7107] [13]
[7108] With the evidence of Annie, as to the candle grease on the floor, and[7109] as to seeing the prisoner take the coffee into the boudoir, the[7110] proceedings were adjourned until the following day.[7111] [13]
[7112] As we went home, Mary Cavendish spoke bitterly against the prosecuting[7113] counsel.[7114] [13]
[7115] “That hateful man! What a net he has drawn around my poor John! How he[7116] twisted every little fact until he made it seem what it wasn’t!”[7117] [13]
[7118] “Well,” I said consolingly, “it will be the other way about to-morrow.”[7119] [13]
[7120] “Yes,” she said meditatively; then suddenly dropped her voice. “Mr.[7121] Hastings, you do not think—surely it could not have been Lawrence—Oh,[7122] no, that could not be!”[7123] [13]
[7124] But I myself was puzzled, and as soon as I was alone with Poirot I[7125] asked him what he thought Sir Ernest was driving at.[7126] [13]
[7127] “Ah!” said Poirot appreciatively. “He is a clever man, that Sir[7128] Ernest.”[7129] [13]
[7130] “Do you think he believes Lawrence guilty?”[7131] [13]
[7132] “I do not think he believes or cares anything! No, what he is trying[7133] for is to create such confusion in the minds of the jury that they are[7134] divided in their opinion as to which brother did it. He is endeavouring[7135] to make out that there is quite as much evidence against Lawrence as[7136] against John—and I am not at all sure that he will not succeed.”[7137] [13]
[7138] Detective-inspector Japp was the first witness called when the trial[7139] was reopened, and gave his evidence succinctly and briefly. After[7140] relating the earlier events, he proceeded:[7141] [13]
[7142] “Acting on information received, Superintendent Summerhaye and myself[7143] searched the prisoner’s room, during his temporary absence from the[7144] house. In his chest of drawers, hidden beneath some underclothing, we[7145] found: first, a pair of gold-rimmed pince-nez similar to those worn by[7146] Mr. Inglethorp”—these were exhibited—“secondly, this phial.”[7147] [13]
[7148] The phial was that already recognized by the chemist’s assistant, a[7149] tiny bottle of blue glass, containing a few grains of a white[7150] crystalline powder, and labelled: “Strychnine Hydro-chloride. POISON.”[7151] [13]
[7152] A fresh piece of evidence discovered by the detectives since the police[7153] court proceedings was a long, almost new piece of blotting-paper. It[7154] had been found in Mrs. Inglethorp’s cheque book, and on being reversed[7155] at a mirror, showed clearly the words: “. . . erything of which I die[7156] possessed I leave to my beloved husband Alfred Ing...” This placed[7157] beyond question the fact that the destroyed will had been in favour of[7158] the deceased lady’s husband. Japp then produced the charred fragment of[7159] paper recovered from the grate, and this, with the discovery of the[7160] beard in the attic, completed his evidence.[7161] [13]
[7162] But Sir Ernest’s cross-examination was yet to come.[7163] [13]
[7164] “What day was it when you searched the prisoner’s room?”[7165] [13]
[7166] “Tuesday, the 24th of July.”[7167] [13]
[7168] “Exactly a week after the tragedy?”[7169] [13]
[7170] “Yes.”[7171] [13]
[7172] “You found these two objects, you say, in the chest of drawers. Was the[7173] drawer unlocked?”[7174] [13]
[7175] “Yes.”[7176] [13]
[7177] “Does it not strike you as unlikely that a man who had committed a[7178] crime should keep the evidence of it in an unlocked drawer for anyone[7179] to find?”[7180] [13]
[7181] “He might have stowed them there in a hurry.”[7182] [13]
[7183] “But you have just said it was a whole week since the crime. He would[7184] have had ample time to remove them and destroy them.”[7185] [13]
[7186] “Perhaps.”[7187] [13]
[7188] “There is no perhaps about it. Would he, or would he not have had[7189] plenty of time to remove and destroy them?”[7190] [13]
[7191] “Yes.”[7192] [13]
[7193] “Was the pile of underclothes under which the things were hidden heavy[7194] or light?”[7195] [13]
[7196] “Heavyish.”[7197] [13]
[7198] “In other words, it was winter underclothing. Obviously, the prisoner[7199] would not be likely to go to that drawer?”[7200] [13]
[7201] “Perhaps not.”[7202] [13]
[7203] “Kindly answer my question. Would the prisoner, in the hottest week of[7204] a hot summer, be likely to go to a drawer containing winter[7205] underclothing. Yes, or no?”[7206] [13]
[7207] “No.”[7208] [13]
[7209] “In that case, is it not possible that the articles in question might[7210] have been put there by a third person, and that the prisoner was quite[7211] unaware of their presence?”[7212] [13]
[7213] “I should not think it likely.”[7214] [13]
[7215] “But it is possible?”[7216] [13]
[7217] “Yes.”[7218] [13]
[7219] “That is all.”[7220] [13]
[7221] More evidence followed. Evidence as to the financial difficulties in[7222] which the prisoner had found himself at the end of July. Evidence as to[7223] his intrigue with Mrs. Raikes—poor Mary, that must have been bitter[7224] hearing for a woman of her pride. Evelyn Howard had been right in her[7225] facts, though her animosity against Alfred Inglethorp had caused her to[7226] jump to the conclusion that he was the person concerned.[7227] [13]
[7228] Lawrence Cavendish was then put into the box. In a low voice, in answer[7229] to Mr. Philips’ questions, he denied having ordered anything from[7230] Parkson’s in June. In fact, on June 29th, he had been staying away, in[7231] Wales.[7232] [13]
[7233] Instantly, Sir Ernest’s chin was shooting pugnaciously forward.[7234] [13]
[7235] “You deny having ordered a black beard from Parkson’s on June 29th?”[7236] [13]
[7237] “I do.”[7238] [13]
[7239] “Ah! In the event of anything happening to your brother, who will[7240] inherit Styles Court?”[7241] [13]
[7242] The brutality of the question called a flush to Lawrence’s pale face.[7243] The judge gave vent to a faint murmur of disapprobation, and the[7244] prisoner in the dock leant forward angrily.[7245] [13]
[7246] Heavywether cared nothing for his client’s anger.[7247] [13]
[7248] “Answer my question, if you please.”[7249] [13]
[7250] “I suppose,” said Lawrence quietly, “that I should.”[7251] [13]
[7252] “What do you mean by you ‘suppose’? Your brother has no children. You[7253] _would_ inherit it, wouldn’t you?”[7254] [13]
[7255] “Yes.”[7256] [13]
[7257] “Ah, that’s better,” said Heavywether, with ferocious geniality. “And[7258] you’d inherit a good slice of money too, wouldn’t you?”[7259] [13]
[7260] “Really, Sir Ernest,” protested the judge, “these questions are not[7261] relevant.”[7262] [13]
[7263] Sir Ernest bowed, and having shot his arrow proceeded.[7264] [13]
[7265] “On Tuesday, the 17th July, you went, I believe, with another guest, to[7266] visit the dispensary at the Red Cross Hospital in Tadminster?”[7267] [13]
[7268] “Yes.”[7269] [13]
[7270] “Did you—while you happened to be alone for a few seconds—unlock the[7271] poison cupboard, and examine some of the bottles?”[7272] [13]
[7273] “I—I—may have done so.”[7274] [13]
[7275] “I put it to you that you did do so?”[7276] [13]
[7277] “Yes.”[7278] [13]
[7279] Sir Ernest fairly shot the next question at him.[7280] [13]
[7281] “Did you examine one bottle in particular?”[7282] [13]
[7283] “No, I do not think so.”[7284] [13]
[7285] “Be careful, Mr. Cavendish. I am referring to a little bottle of[7286] Hydro-chloride of Strychnine.”[7287] [13]
[7288] Lawrence was turning a sickly greenish colour.[7289] [13]
[7290] “N—o—I am sure I didn’t.”[7291] [13]
[7292] “Then how do you account for the fact that you left the unmistakable[7293] impress of your finger-prints on it?”[7294] [13]
[7295] The bullying manner was highly efficacious with a nervous disposition.[7296] [13]
[7297] “I—I suppose I must have taken up the bottle.”[7298] [13]
[7299] “I suppose so too! Did you abstract any of the contents of the bottle?”[7300] [13]
[7301] “Certainly not.”[7302] [13]
[7303] “Then why did you take it up?”[7304] [13]
[7305] “I once studied to be a doctor. Such things naturally interest me.”[7306] [13]
[7307] “Ah! So poisons ‘naturally interest’ you, do they? Still, you waited to[7308] be alone before gratifying that ‘interest’ of yours?”[7309] [13]
[7310] “That was pure chance. If the others had been there, I should have done[7311] just the same.”[7312] [13]
[7313] “Still, as it happens, the others were not there?”[7314] [13]
[7315] “No, but——”[7316] [13]
[7317] “In fact, during the whole afternoon, you were only alone for a couple[7318] of minutes, and it happened—I say, it happened—to be during those two[7319] minutes that you displayed your ‘natural interest’ in Hydro-chloride of[7320] Strychnine?”[7321] [13]
[7322] Lawrence stammered pitiably.[7323] [13]
[7324] “I—I——”[7325] [13]
[7326] With a satisfied and expressive countenance, Sir Ernest observed:[7327] [13]
[7328] “I have nothing more to ask you, Mr. Cavendish.”[7329] [13]
[7330] This bit of cross-examination had caused great excitement in court. The[7331] heads of the many fashionably attired women present were busily laid[7332] together, and their whispers became so loud that the judge angrily[7333] threatened to have the court cleared if there was not immediate[7334] silence.[7335] [13]
[7336] There was little more evidence. The hand-writing experts were called[7337] upon for their opinion of the signature of “Alfred Inglethorp” in the[7338] chemist’s poison register. They all declared unanimously that it was[7339] certainly not his hand-writing, and gave it as their view that it might[7340] be that of the prisoner disguised. Cross-examined, they admitted that[7341] it might be the prisoner’s hand-writing cleverly counterfeited.[7342] [13]
[7343] Sir Ernest Heavywether’s speech in opening the case for the defence was[7344] not a long one, but it was backed by the full force of his emphatic[7345] manner. Never, he said, in the course of his long experience, had he[7346] known a charge of murder rest on slighter evidence. Not only was it[7347] entirely circumstantial, but the greater part of it was practically[7348] unproved. Let them take the testimony they had heard and sift it[7349] impartially. The strychnine had been found in a drawer in the[7350] prisoner’s room. That drawer was an unlocked one, as he had pointed[7351] out, and he submitted that there was no evidence to prove that it was[7352] the prisoner who had concealed the poison there. It was, in fact, a[7353] wicked and malicious attempt on the part of some third person to fix[7354] the crime on the prisoner. The prosecution had been unable to produce a[7355] shred of evidence in support of their contention that it was the[7356] prisoner who ordered the black beard from Parkson’s. The quarrel which[7357] had taken place between prisoner and his stepmother was freely[7358] admitted, but both it and his financial embarrassments had been grossly[7359] exaggerated.[7360] [13]
[7361] His learned friend—Sir Ernest nodded carelessly at Mr. Philips—had[7362] stated that if the prisoner were an innocent man, he would have come[7363] forward at the inquest to explain that it was he, and not Mr.[7364] Inglethorp, who had been the participator in the quarrel. He thought[7365] the facts had been misrepresented. What had actually occurred was this.[7366] The prisoner, returning to the house on Tuesday evening, had been[7367] authoritatively told that there had been a violent quarrel between Mr.[7368] and Mrs. Inglethorp. No suspicion had entered the prisoner’s head that[7369] anyone could possibly have mistaken his voice for that of Mr.[7370] Inglethorp. He naturally concluded that his stepmother had had two[7371] quarrels.[7372] [13]
[7373] The prosecution averred that on Monday, July 16th, the prisoner had[7374] entered the chemist’s shop in the village, disguised as Mr. Inglethorp.[7375] The prisoner, on the contrary, was at that time at a lonely spot called[7376] Marston’s Spinney, where he had been summoned by an anonymous note,[7377] couched in blackmailing terms, and threatening to reveal certain[7378] matters to his wife unless he complied with its demands. The prisoner[7379] had, accordingly, gone to the appointed spot, and after waiting there[7380] vainly for half an hour had returned home. Unfortunately, he had met[7381] with no one on the way there or back who could vouch for the truth of[7382] his story, but luckily he had kept the note, and it would be produced[7383] as evidence.[7384] [13]
[7385] As for the statement relating to the destruction of the will, the[7386] prisoner had formerly practised at the Bar, and was perfectly well[7387] aware that the will made in his favour a year before was automatically[7388] revoked by his stepmother’s remarriage. He would call evidence to show[7389] who did destroy the will, and it was possible that that might open up[7390] quite a new view of the case.[7391] [13]
[7392] Finally, he would point out to the jury that there was evidence against[7393] other people besides John Cavendish. He would direct their attention to[7394] the fact that the evidence against Mr. Lawrence Cavendish was quite as[7395] strong, if not stronger than that against his brother.[7396] [13]
[7397] He would now call the prisoner.[7398] [13]
[7399] John acquitted himself well in the witness-box. Under Sir Ernest’s[7400] skilful handling, he told his tale credibly and well. The anonymous[7401] note received by him was produced, and handed to the jury to examine.[7402] The readiness with which he admitted his financial difficulties, and[7403] the disagreement with his stepmother, lent value to his denials.[7404] [13]
[7405] At the close of his examination, he paused, and said:[7406] [13]
[7407] “I should like to make one thing clear. I utterly reject and disapprove[7408] of Sir Ernest Heavywether’s insinuations against my brother. My[7409] brother, I am convinced, had no more to do with the crime than I have.”[7410] [13]
[7411] Sir Ernest merely smiled, and noted with a sharp eye that John’s[7412] protest had produced a very favourable impression on the jury.[7413] [13]
[7414] Then the cross-examination began.[7415] [13]
[7416] “I understand you to say that it never entered your head that the[7417] witnesses at the inquest could possibly have mistaken your voice for[7418] that of Mr. Inglethorp. Is not that very surprising?”[7419] [13]
[7420] “No, I don’t think so. I was told there had been a quarrel between my[7421] mother and Mr. Inglethorp, and it never occurred to me that such was[7422] not really the case.”[7423] [13]
[7424] “Not when the servant Dorcas repeated certain fragments of the[7425] conversation—fragments which you must have recognized?”[7426] [13]
[7427] “I did not recognize them.”[7428] [13]
[7429] “Your memory must be unusually short!”[7430] [13]
[7431] “No, but we were both angry, and, I think, said more than we meant. I[7432] paid very little attention to my mother’s actual words.”[7433] [13]
[7434] Mr. Philips’ incredulous sniff was a triumph of forensic skill. He[7435] passed on to the subject of the note.[7436] [13]
[7437] “You have produced this note very opportunely. Tell me, is there[7438] nothing familiar about the hand-writing of it?”[7439] [13]
[7440] “Not that I know of.”[7441] [13]
[7442] “Do you not think that it bears a marked resemblance to your own[7443] hand-writing—carelessly disguised?”[7444] [13]
[7445] “No, I do not think so.”[7446] [13]
[7447] “I put it to you that it is your own hand-writing!”[7448] [13]
[7449] “No.”[7450] [13]
[7451] “I put it to you that, anxious to prove an alibi, you conceived the[7452] idea of a fictitious and rather incredible appointment, and wrote this[7453] note yourself in order to bear out your statement!”[7454] [13]
[7455] “No.”[7456] [13]
[7457] “Is it not a fact that, at the time you claim to have been waiting[7458] about at a solitary and unfrequented spot, you were really in the[7459] chemist’s shop in Styles St. Mary, where you purchased strychnine in[7460] the name of Alfred Inglethorp?”[7461] [13]
[7462] “No, that is a lie.”[7463] [13]
[7464] “I put it to you that, wearing a suit of Mr. Inglethorp’s clothes, with[7465] a black beard trimmed to resemble his, you were there—and signed the[7466] register in his name!”[7467] [13]
[7468] “That is absolutely untrue.”[7469] [13]
[7470] “Then I will leave the remarkable similarity of hand-writing between[7471] the note, the register, and your own, to the consideration of the[7472] jury,” said Mr. Philips, and sat down with the air of a man who has[7473] done his duty, but who was nevertheless horrified by such deliberate[7474] perjury.[7475] [13]
[7476] After this, as it was growing late, the case was adjourned till Monday.[7477] [13]
[7478] Poirot, I noticed, was looking profoundly discouraged. He had that[7479] little frown between the eyes that I knew so well.[7480] [13]
[7481] “What is it, Poirot?” I inquired.[7482] [13]
[7483] “Ah, _mon ami_, things are going badly, badly.”[7484] [13]
[7485] In spite of myself, my heart gave a leap of relief. Evidently there was[7486] a likelihood of John Cavendish being acquitted.[7487] [13]
[7488] When we reached the house, my little friend waved aside Mary’s offer of[7489] tea.[7490] [13]
[7491] “No, I thank you, madame. I will mount to my room.”[7492] [13]
[7493] I followed him. Still frowning, he went across to the desk and took out[7494] a small pack of patience cards. Then he drew up a chair to the table,[7495] and, to my utter amazement, began solemnly to build card houses![7496] [13]
[7497] My jaw dropped involuntarily, and he said at once:[7498] [13]
[7499] “No, _mon ami_, I am not in my second childhood! I steady my nerves,[7500] that is all. This employment requires precision of the fingers. With[7501] precision of the fingers goes precision of the brain. And never have I[7502] needed that more than now!”[7503] [13]
[7504] “What is the trouble?” I asked.[7505] [13]
[7506] With a great thump on the table, Poirot demolished his carefully built[7507] up edifice.[7508] [13]
[7509] “It is this, _mon ami!_ That I can build card houses seven stories[7510] high, but I cannot”—thump—“find”—thump—“ that last link of which I[7511] spoke to you.”[7512] [13]
[7513] I could not quite tell what to say, so I held my peace, and he began[7514] slowly building up the cards again, speaking in jerks as he did so.[7515] [13]
[7516] “It is done—so! By placing—one card—on another—with[7517] mathematical—precision!”[7518] [13]
[7519] I watched the card house rising under his hands, story by story. He[7520] never hesitated or faltered. It was really almost like a conjuring[7521] trick.[7522] [13]
[7523] “What a steady hand you’ve got,” I remarked. “I believe I’ve only seen[7524] your hand shake once.”[7525] [13]
[7526] “On an occasion when I was enraged, without doubt,” observed Poirot,[7527] with great placidity.[7528] [13]
[7529] “Yes indeed! You were in a towering rage. Do you remember? It was when[7530] you discovered that the lock of the despatch-case in Mrs. Inglethorp’s[7531] bedroom had been forced. You stood by the mantelpiece, twiddling the[7532] things on it in your usual fashion, and your hand shook like a leaf! I[7533] must say——”[7534] [13]
[7535] But I stopped suddenly. For Poirot, uttering a hoarse and inarticulate[7536] cry, again annihilated his masterpiece of cards, and putting his hands[7537] over his eyes swayed backwards and forwards, apparently suffering the[7538] keenest agony.[7539] [13]
[7540] “Good heavens, Poirot!” I cried. “What is the matter? Are you taken[7541] ill?”[7542] [13]
[7543] “No, no,” he gasped. “It is—it is—that I have an idea!”[7544] [13]
[7545] “Oh!” I exclaimed, much relieved. “One of your ‘little ideas’?”[7546] [13]
[7547] “Ah, _ma foi_, no!” replied Poirot frankly. “This time it is an idea[7548] gigantic! Stupendous! And you—_you_, my friend, have given it to me!”[7549] [13]
[7550] Suddenly clasping me in his arms, he kissed me warmly on both cheeks,[7551] and before I had recovered from my surprise ran headlong from the room.[7552] [13]
[7553] Mary Cavendish entered at that moment.[7554] [13]
[7555] “What _is_ the matter with Monsieur Poirot? He rushed past me crying[7556] out: ‘A garage! For the love of Heaven, direct me to a garage, madame!’[7557] And, before I could answer, he had dashed out into the street.”[7558] [13]
[7559] I hurried to the window. True enough, there he was, tearing down the[7560] street, hatless, and gesticulating as he went. I turned to Mary with a[7561] gesture of despair.[7562] [13]
[7563] “He’ll be stopped by a policeman in another minute. There he goes,[7564] round the corner!”[7565] [13]
[7566] Our eyes met, and we stared helplessly at one another.[7567] [13]
[7568] “What can be the matter?”[7569] [13]
[7570] I shook my head.[7571] [13]
[7572] “I don’t know. He was building card houses, when suddenly he said he[7573] had an idea, and rushed off as you saw.”[7574] [13]
[7575] “Well,” said Mary, “I expect he will be back before dinner.”[7576] [13]
[7577] But night fell, and Poirot had not returned.[7578] [13]
[7579] [13]
[7580] [13]
[7581] [13]
[7582] CHAPTER XII.[7583] THE LAST LINK[7584] [13]
[7585] [13]
[7586] Poirot’s abrupt departure had intrigued us all greatly. Sunday morning[7587] wore away, and still he did not reappear. But about three o’clock a[7588] ferocious and prolonged hooting outside drove us to the window, to see[7589] Poirot alighting from a car, accompanied by Japp and Summerhaye. The[7590] little man was transformed. He radiated an absurd complacency. He bowed[7591] with exaggerated respect to Mary Cavendish.[7592] [13]
[7593] “Madame, I have your permission to hold a little _réunion_ in the[7594] _salon_? It is necessary for everyone to attend.”[7595] [13]
[7596] Mary smiled sadly.[7597] [13]
[7598] “You know, Monsieur Poirot, that you have _carte blanche_ in every[7599] way.”[7600] [13]
[7601] “You are too amiable, madame.”[7602] [13]
[7603] Still beaming, Poirot marshalled us all into the drawing-room, bringing[7604] forward chairs as he did so.[7605] [13]
[7606] “Miss Howard—here. Mademoiselle Cynthia. Monsieur Lawrence. The good[7607] Dorcas. And Annie. _Bien!_ We must delay our proceedings a few minutes[7608] until Mr. Inglethorp arrives. I have sent him a note.”[7609] [13]
[7610] Miss Howard rose immediately from her seat.[7611] [13]
[7612] “If that man comes into the house, I leave it!”[7613] [13]
[7614] “No, no!” Poirot went up to her and pleaded in a low voice.[7615] [13]
[7616] Finally Miss Howard consented to return to her chair. A few minutes[7617] later Alfred Inglethorp entered the room.[7618] [13]
[7619] The company once assembled, Poirot rose from his seat with the air of a[7620] popular lecturer, and bowed politely to his audience.[7621] [13]
[7622] “_Messieurs, mesdames_, as you all know, I was called in by Monsieur[7623] John Cavendish to investigate this case. I at once examined the bedroom[7624] of the deceased which, by the advice of the doctors, had been kept[7625] locked, and was consequently exactly as it had been when the tragedy[7626] occurred. I found: first, a fragment of green material; second, a stain[7627] on the carpet near the window, still damp; thirdly, an empty box of[7628] bromide powders.[7629] [13]
[7630] “To take the fragment of green material first, I found it caught in the[7631] bolt of the communicating door between that room and the adjoining one[7632] occupied by Mademoiselle Cynthia. I handed the fragment over to the[7633] police who did not consider it of much importance. Nor did they[7634] recognize it for what it was—a piece torn from a green land armlet.”[7635] [13]
[7636] There was a little stir of excitement.[7637] [13]
[7638] “Now there was only one person at Styles who worked on the land—Mrs.[7639] Cavendish. Therefore it must have been Mrs. Cavendish who entered the[7640] deceased’s room through the door communicating with Mademoiselle[7641] Cynthia’s room.”[7642] [13]
[7643] “But that door was bolted on the inside!” I cried.[7644] [13]
[7645] “When I examined the room, yes. But in the first place we have only her[7646] word for it, since it was she who tried that particular door and[7647] reported it fastened. In the ensuing confusion she would have had ample[7648] opportunity to shoot the bolt across. I took an early opportunity of[7649] verifying my conjectures. To begin with, the fragment corresponds[7650] exactly with a tear in Mrs. Cavendish’s armlet. Also, at the inquest,[7651] Mrs. Cavendish declared that she had heard, from her own room, the fall[7652] of the table by the bed. I took an early opportunity of testing that[7653] statement by stationing my friend Monsieur Hastings in the left wing of[7654] the building, just outside Mrs. Cavendish’s door. I myself, in company[7655] with the police, went to the deceased’s room, and whilst there I,[7656] apparently accidentally, knocked over the table in question, but found[7657] that, as I had expected, Monsieur Hastings had heard no sound at all.[7658] This confirmed my belief that Mrs. Cavendish was not speaking the truth[7659] when she declared that she had been dressing in her room at the time of[7660] the tragedy. In fact, I was convinced that, far from having been in her[7661] own room, Mrs. Cavendish was actually in the deceased’s room when the[7662] alarm was given.”[7663] [13]
[7664] I shot a quick glance at Mary. She was very pale, but smiling.[7665] [13]
[7666] “I proceeded to reason on that assumption. Mrs. Cavendish is in her[7667] mother-in-law’s room. We will say that she is seeking for something and[7668] has not yet found it. Suddenly Mrs. Inglethorp awakens and is seized[7669] with an alarming paroxysm. She flings out her arm, overturning the bed[7670] table, and then pulls desperately at the bell. Mrs. Cavendish,[7671] startled, drops her candle, scattering the grease on the carpet. She[7672] picks it up, and retreats quickly to Mademoiselle Cynthia’s room,[7673] closing the door behind her. She hurries out into the passage, for the[7674] servants must not find her where she is. But it is too late! Already[7675] footsteps are echoing along the gallery which connects the two wings.[7676] What can she do? Quick as thought, she hurries back to the young girl’s[7677] room, and starts shaking her awake. The hastily aroused household come[7678] trooping down the passage. They are all busily battering at Mrs.[7679] Inglethorp’s door. It occurs to nobody that Mrs. Cavendish has not[7680] arrived with the rest, but—and this is significant—I can find no one[7681] who saw her come from the other wing.” He looked at Mary Cavendish. “Am[7682] I right, madame?”[7683] [13]
[7684] She bowed her head.[7685] [13]
[7686] “Quite right, monsieur. You understand that, if I had thought I would[7687] do my husband any good by revealing these facts, I would have done so.[7688] But it did not seem to me to bear upon the question of his guilt or[7689] innocence.”[7690] [13]
[7691] “In a sense, that is correct, madame. But it cleared my mind of many[7692] misconceptions, and left me free to see other facts in their true[7693] significance.”[7694] [13]
[7695] “The will!” cried Lawrence. “Then it was you, Mary, who destroyed the[7696] will?”[7697] [13]
[7698] She shook her head, and Poirot shook his also.[7699] [13]
[7700] “No,” he said quietly. “There is only one person who could possibly[7701] have destroyed that will—Mrs. Inglethorp herself!”[7702] [13]
[7703] “Impossible!” I exclaimed. “She had only made it out that very[7704] afternoon!”[7705] [13]
[7706] “Nevertheless, _mon ami_, it was Mrs. Inglethorp. Because, in no other[7707] way can you account for the fact that, on one of the hottest days of[7708] the year, Mrs. Inglethorp ordered a fire to be lighted in her room.”[7709] [13]
[7710] I gave a gasp. What idiots we had been never to think of that fire as[7711] being incongruous! Poirot was continuing:[7712] [13]
[7713] “The temperature on that day, messieurs, was 80 degrees in the shade.[7714] Yet Mrs. Inglethorp ordered a fire! Why? Because she wished to destroy[7715] something, and could think of no other way. You will remember that, in[7716] consequence of the War economics practiced at Styles, no waste paper[7717] was thrown away. There was therefore no means of destroying a thick[7718] document such as a will. The moment I heard of a fire being lighted in[7719] Mrs. Inglethorp’s room, I leaped to the conclusion that it was to[7720] destroy some important document—possibly a will. So the discovery of[7721] the charred fragment in the grate was no surprise to me. I did not, of[7722] course, know at the time that the will in question had only been made[7723] this afternoon, and I will admit that, when I learnt that fact, I fell[7724] into a grievous error. I came to the conclusion that Mrs. Inglethorp’s[7725] determination to destroy her will arose as a direct consequence of the[7726] quarrel she had that afternoon, and that therefore the quarrel took[7727] place after, and not before the making of the will.[7728] [13]
[7729] “Here, as we know, I was wrong, and I was forced to abandon that idea.[7730] I faced the problem from a new standpoint. Now, at four o’clock, Dorcas[7731] overheard her mistress saying angrily: ‘You need not think that any[7732] fear of publicity, or scandal between husband and wife will deter me.”[7733] I conjectured, and conjectured rightly, that these words were[7734] addressed, not to her husband, but to Mr. John Cavendish.
