At five[7735] o’clock, an hour later, she uses almost the same words, but the[7736] standpoint is different. She admits to Dorcas, ‘I don’t know what to[7737] do; scandal between husband and wife is a dreadful thing.’ At four[7738] o’clock she has been angry, but completely mistress of herself. At five[7739] o’clock she is in violent distress, and speaks of having had a great[7740] shock.[7741] [13]
[7742] “Looking at the matter psychologically, I drew one deduction which I[7743] was convinced was correct. The second ‘scandal’ she spoke of was not[7744] the same as the first—and it concerned herself![7745] [13]
[7746] “Let us reconstruct. At four o’clock, Mrs. Inglethorp quarrels with her[7747] son, and threatens to denounce him to his wife—who, by the way,[7748] overheard the greater part of the conversation. At four-thirty, Mrs.[7749] Inglethorp, in consequence of a conversation on the validity of wills,[7750] makes a will in favour of her husband, which the two gardeners witness.[7751] At five o’clock, Dorcas finds her mistress in a state of considerable[7752] agitation, with a slip of paper—‘a letter,’ Dorcas thinks—in her hand,[7753] and it is then that she orders the fire in her room to be lighted.[7754] Presumably, then, between four-thirty and five o’clock, something has[7755] occurred to occasion a complete revolution of feeling, since she is now[7756] as anxious to destroy the will, as she was before to make it. What was[7757] that something?[7758] [13]
[7759] “As far as we know, she was quite alone during that half-hour. Nobody[7760] entered or left that boudoir. What then occasioned this sudden change[7761] of sentiment?[7762] [13]
[7763] “One can only guess, but I believe my guess to be correct. Mrs.[7764] Inglethorp had no stamps in her desk. We know this, because later she[7765] asked Dorcas to bring her some. Now in the opposite corner of the room[7766] stood her husband’s desk—locked. She was anxious to find some stamps,[7767] and, according to my theory, she tried her own keys in the desk. That[7768] one of them fitted I know. She therefore opened the desk, and in[7769] searching for the stamps she came across something else—that slip of[7770] paper which Dorcas saw in her hand, and which assuredly was never meant[7771] for Mrs. Inglethorp’s eyes. On the other hand, Mrs. Cavendish believed[7772] that the slip of paper to which her mother-in-law clung so tenaciously[7773] was a written proof of her own husband’s infidelity. She demanded it[7774] from Mrs. Inglethorp who assured her, quite truly, that it had nothing[7775] to do with that matter. Mrs. Cavendish did not believe her. She thought[7776] that Mrs. Inglethorp was shielding her stepson. Now Mrs. Cavendish is a[7777] very resolute woman, and, behind her mask of reserve, she was madly[7778] jealous of her husband. She determined to get hold of that paper at all[7779] costs, and in this resolution chance came to her aid. She happened to[7780] pick up the key of Mrs. Inglethorp’s despatch-case, which had been lost[7781] that morning. She knew that her mother-in-law invariably kept all[7782] important papers in this particular case.[7783] [13]
[7784] “Mrs. Cavendish, therefore, made her plans as only a woman driven[7785] desperate through jealousy could have done. Some time in the evening[7786] she unbolted the door leading into Mademoiselle Cynthia’s room.[7787] Possibly she applied oil to the hinges, for I found that it opened[7788] quite noiselessly when I tried it. She put off her project until the[7789] early hours of the morning as being safer, since the servants were[7790] accustomed to hearing her move about her room at that time. She dressed[7791] completely in her land kit, and made her way quietly through[7792] Mademoiselle Cynthia’s room into that of Mrs. Inglethorp.”[7793] [13]
[7794] He paused a moment, and Cynthia interrupted:[7795] [13]
[7796] “But I should have woken up if anyone had come through my room?”[7797] [13]
[7798] “Not if you were drugged, mademoiselle.”[7799] [13]
[7800] “Drugged?”[7801] [13]
[7802] “_Mais, oui!_”[7803] [13]
[7804] “You remember”—he addressed us collectively again—“that through all the[7805] tumult and noise next door Mademoiselle Cynthia slept. That admitted of[7806] two possibilities. Either her sleep was feigned—which I did not[7807] believe—or her unconsciousness was induced by artificial means.[7808] [13]
[7809] “With this latter idea in my mind, I examined all the coffee-cups most[7810] carefully, remembering that it was Mrs. Cavendish who had brought[7811] Mademoiselle Cynthia her coffee the night before. I took a sample from[7812] each cup, and had them analysed—with no result. I had counted the cups[7813] carefully, in the event of one having been removed. Six persons had[7814] taken coffee, and six cups were duly found. I had to confess myself[7815] mistaken.[7816] [13]
[7817] “Then I discovered that I had been guilty of a very grave oversight.[7818] Coffee had been brought in for seven persons, not six, for Dr.[7819] Bauerstein had been there that evening. This changed the face of the[7820] whole affair, for there was now one cup missing. The servants noticed[7821] nothing, since Annie, the housemaid, who took in the coffee, brought in[7822] seven cups, not knowing that Mr. Inglethorp never drank it, whereas[7823] Dorcas, who cleared them away the following morning, found six as[7824] usual—or strictly speaking she found five, the sixth being the one[7825] found broken in Mrs. Inglethorp’s room.[7826] [13]
[7827] “I was confident that the missing cup was that of Mademoiselle Cynthia.[7828] I had an additional reason for that belief in the fact that all the[7829] cups found contained sugar, which Mademoiselle Cynthia never took in[7830] her coffee. My attention was attracted by the story of Annie about some[7831] ‘salt’ on the tray of cocoa which she took every night to Mrs.[7832] Inglethorp’s room. I accordingly secured a sample of that cocoa, and[7833] sent it to be analysed.”[7834] [13]
[7835] “But that had already been done by Dr. Bauerstein,” said Lawrence[7836] quickly.[7837] [13]
[7838] “Not exactly. The analyst was asked by him to report whether strychnine[7839] was, or was not, present. He did not have it tested, as I did, for a[7840] narcotic.”[7841] [13]
[7842] “For a narcotic?”[7843] [13]
[7844] “Yes. Here is the analyst’s report. Mrs. Cavendish administered a safe,[7845] but effectual, narcotic to both Mrs. Inglethorp and Mademoiselle[7846] Cynthia. And it is possible that she had a _mauvais quart d’heure_ in[7847] consequence! Imagine her feelings when her mother-in-law is suddenly[7848] taken ill and dies, and immediately after she hears the word ‘Poison’![7849] She has believed that the sleeping draught she administered was[7850] perfectly harmless, but there is no doubt that for one terrible moment[7851] she must have feared that Mrs. Inglethorp’s death lay at her door. She[7852] is seized with panic, and under its influence she hurries downstairs,[7853] and quickly drops the coffee-cup and saucer used by Mademoiselle[7854] Cynthia into a large brass vase, where it is discovered later by[7855] Monsieur Lawrence. The remains of the cocoa she dare not touch. Too[7856] many eyes are upon her. Guess at her relief when strychnine is[7857] mentioned, and she discovers that after all the tragedy is not her[7858] doing.[7859] [13]
[7860] “We are now able to account for the symptoms of strychnine poisoning[7861] being so long in making their appearance. A narcotic taken with[7862] strychnine will delay the action of the poison for some hours.”[7863] [13]
[7864] Poirot paused. Mary looked up at him, the colour slowly rising in her[7865] face.[7866] [13]
[7867] “All you have said is quite true, Monsieur Poirot. It was the most[7868] awful hour of my life. I shall never forget it. But you are wonderful.[7869] I understand now——”[7870] [13]
[7871] “What I meant when I told you that you could safely confess to Papa[7872] Poirot, eh? But you would not trust me.”[7873] [13]
[7874] “I see everything now,” said Lawrence. “The drugged cocoa, taken on top[7875] of the poisoned coffee, amply accounts for the delay.”[7876] [13]
[7877] “Exactly. But was the coffee poisoned, or was it not? We come to a[7878] little difficulty here, since Mrs. Inglethorp never drank it.”[7879] [13]
[7880] “What?” The cry of surprise was universal.[7881] [13]
[7882] “No. You will remember my speaking of a stain on the carpet in Mrs.[7883] Inglethorp’s room? There were some peculiar points about that stain. It[7884] was still damp, it exhaled a strong odour of coffee, and imbedded in[7885] the nap of the carpet I found some little splinters of china. What had[7886] happened was plain to me, for not two minutes before I had placed my[7887] little case on the table near the window, and the table, tilting up,[7888] had deposited it upon the floor on precisely the identical spot. In[7889] exactly the same way, Mrs. Inglethorp had laid down her cup of coffee[7890] on reaching her room the night before, and the treacherous table had[7891] played her the same trick.[7892] [13]
[7893] “What happened next is mere guess work on my part, but I should say[7894] that Mrs. Inglethorp picked up the broken cup and placed it on the[7895] table by the bed. Feeling in need of a stimulant of some kind, she[7896] heated up her cocoa, and drank it off then and there. Now we are faced[7897] with a new problem. We know the cocoa contained no strychnine. The[7898] coffee was never drunk. Yet the strychnine must have been administered[7899] between seven and nine o’clock that evening. What third medium was[7900] there—a medium so suitable for disguising the taste of strychnine that[7901] it is extraordinary no one has thought of it?” Poirot looked round the[7902] room, and then answered himself impressively. “Her medicine!”[7903] [13]
[7904] “Do you mean that the murderer introduced the strychnine into her[7905] tonic?” I cried.[7906] [13]
[7907] “There was no need to introduce it. It was already there—in the[7908] mixture. The strychnine that killed Mrs. Inglethorp was the identical[7909] strychnine prescribed by Dr. Wilkins. To make that clear to you, I will[7910] read you an extract from a book on dispensing which I found in the[7911] Dispensary of the Red Cross Hospital at Tadminster:[7912] [13]
[7913] “‘The following prescription has become famous in text books:[7914] [13]
[7915] [13]
[7916]           Strychninae Sulph. . . . . . 1 gr.[7917] [13]
[7918]           Potass Bromide . . . . . . . 3vi[7919] [13]
[7920]           Aqua ad. . . . . . . . . . . . . 3viii[7921] [13]
[7922]           Fiat Mistura[7923] [13]
[7924] [13]
[7925] _This solution deposits in a few hours the greater part of the[7926] strychnine salt as an insoluble bromide in transparent crystals. A lady[7927] in England lost her life by taking a similar mixture: the precipitated[7928] strychnine collected at the bottom, and in taking the last dose she[7929] swallowed nearly all of it!_[7930] [13]
[7931] “Now there was, of course, no bromide in Dr. Wilkins’ prescription, but[7932] you will remember that I mentioned an empty box of bromide powders. One[7933] or two of those powders introduced into the full bottle of medicine[7934] would effectually precipitate the strychnine, as the book describes,[7935] and cause it to be taken in the last dose. You will learn later that[7936] the person who usually poured out Mrs. Inglethorp’s medicine was always[7937] extremely careful not to shake the bottle, but to leave the sediment at[7938] the bottom of it undisturbed.[7939] [13]
[7940] “Throughout the case, there have been evidences that the tragedy was[7941] intended to take place on Monday evening. On that day, Mrs.[7942] Inglethorp’s bell wire was neatly cut, and on Monday evening[7943] Mademoiselle Cynthia was spending the night with friends, so that Mrs.[7944] Inglethorp would have been quite alone in the right wing, completely[7945] shut off from help of any kind, and would have died, in all[7946] probability, before medical aid could have been summoned. But in her[7947] hurry to be in time for the village entertainment Mrs. Inglethorp[7948] forgot to take her medicine, and the next day she lunched away from[7949] home, so that the last—and fatal—dose was actually taken twenty-four[7950] hours later than had been anticipated by the murderer; and it is owing[7951] to that delay that the final proof—the last link of the chain—is now in[7952] my hands.”[7953] [13]
[7954] Amid breathless excitement, he held out three thin strips of paper.[7955] [13]
[7956] “A letter in the murderer’s own hand-writing, _mes amis!_ Had it been a[7957] little clearer in its terms, it is possible that Mrs. Inglethorp,[7958] warned in time, would have escaped. As it was, she realized her danger,[7959] but not the manner of it.”[7960] [13]
[7961] In the deathly silence, Poirot pieced together the slips of paper and,[7962] clearing his throat, read:[7963] [13]
[7964] Dearest Evelyn:[7965] [13]
[7966]     ‘You will be anxious at hearing nothing. It is all right—only it[7967]     will be to-night instead of last night. You understand. There’s a[7968]     good time coming once the old woman is dead and out of the way. No[7969]     one can possibly bring home the crime to me. That idea of yours[7970]     about the bromides was a stroke of genius! But we must be very[7971]     circumspect. A false step——’[7972] [13]
[7973] [13]
[7974] “Here, my friends, the letter breaks off. Doubtless the writer was[7975] interrupted; but there can be no question as to his identity. We all[7976] know this hand-writing and——”[7977] [13]
[7978] A howl that was almost a scream broke the silence.[7979] [13]
[7980] “You devil! How did you get it?”[7981] [13]
[7982] A chair was overturned. Poirot skipped nimbly aside. A quick movement[7983] on his part, and his assailant fell with a crash.[7984] [13]
[7985] “_Messieurs, mesdames_,” said Poirot, with a flourish, “let me[7986] introduce you to the murderer, Mr. Alfred Inglethorp!”[7987] [13]
[7988] [13]
[7989] [13]
[7990] [13]
[7991] CHAPTER XIII.[7992] POIROT EXPLAINS[7993] [13]
[7994] [13]
[7995] “Poirot, you old villain,” I said, “I’ve half a mind to strangle you![7996] What do you mean by deceiving me as you have done?”[7997] [13]
[7998] We were sitting in the library. Several hectic days lay behind us. In[7999] the room below, John and Mary were together once more, while Alfred[8000] Inglethorp and Miss Howard were in custody. Now at last, I had Poirot[8001] to myself, and could relieve my still burning curiosity.[8002] [13]
[8003] Poirot did not answer me for a moment, but at last he said:[8004] [13]
[8005] “I did not deceive you, _mon ami_. At most, I permitted you to deceive[8006] yourself.”[8007] [13]
[8008] “Yes, but why?”[8009] [13]
[8010] “Well, it is difficult to explain. You see, my friend, you have a[8011] nature so honest, and a countenance so transparent, that—_enfin_, to[8012] conceal your feelings is impossible! If I had told you my ideas, the[8013] very first time you saw Mr. Alfred Inglethorp that astute gentleman[8014] would have—in your so expressive idiom—‘smelt a rat’! And then,[8015] _bonjour_ to our chances of catching him!”[8016] [13]
[8017] “I think that I have more diplomacy than you give me credit for.”[8018] [13]
[8019] “My friend,” besought Poirot, “I implore you, do not enrage yourself![8020] Your help has been of the most invaluable. It is but the extremely[8021] beautiful nature that you have, which made me pause.”[8022] [13]
[8023] “Well,” I grumbled, a little mollified. “I still think you might have[8024] given me a hint.”[8025] [13]
[8026] “But I did, my friend. Several hints. You would not take them. Think[8027] now, did I ever say to you that I believed John Cavendish guilty? Did I[8028] not, on the contrary, tell you that he would almost certainly be[8029] acquitted?”[8030] [13]
[8031] “Yes, but——”[8032] [13]
[8033] “And did I not immediately afterwards speak of the difficulty of[8034] bringing the murderer to justice? Was it not plain to you that I was[8035] speaking of two entirely different persons?”[8036] [13]
[8037] “No,” I said, “it was not plain to me!”[8038] [13]
[8039] “Then again,” continued Poirot, “at the beginning, did I not repeat to[8040] you several times that I didn’t want Mr. Inglethorp arrested _now_?[8041] That should have conveyed something to you.”[8042] [13]
[8043] “Do you mean to say you suspected him as long ago as that?”[8044] [13]
[8045] “Yes. To begin with, whoever else might benefit by Mrs. Inglethorp’s[8046] death, her husband would benefit the most. There was no getting away[8047] from that. When I went up to Styles with you that first day, I had no[8048] idea as to how the crime had been committed, but from what I knew of[8049] Mr. Inglethorp I fancied that it would be very hard to find anything to[8050] connect him with it. When I arrived at the château, I realized at once[8051] that it was Mrs. Inglethorp who had burnt the will; and there, by the[8052] way, you cannot complain, my friend, for I tried my best to force on[8053] you the significance of that bedroom fire in midsummer.”[8054] [13]
[8055] “Yes, yes,” I said impatiently. “Go on.”[8056] [13]
[8057] “Well, my friend, as I say, my views as to Mr. Inglethorp’s guilt were[8058] very much shaken. There was, in fact, so much evidence against him that[8059] I was inclined to believe that he had not done it.”[8060] [13]
[8061] “When did you change your mind?”[8062] [13]
[8063] “When I found that the more efforts I made to clear him, the more[8064] efforts he made to get himself arrested. Then, when I discovered that[8065] Inglethorp had nothing to do with Mrs. Raikes and that in fact it was[8066] John Cavendish who was interested in that quarter, I was quite sure.”[8067] [13]
[8068] “But why?”[8069] [13]
[8070] “Simply this. If it had been Inglethorp who was carrying on an intrigue[8071] with Mrs. Raikes, his silence was perfectly comprehensible. But, when I[8072] discovered that it was known all over the village that it was John who[8073] was attracted by the farmer’s pretty wife, his silence bore quite a[8074] different interpretation. It was nonsense to pretend that he was afraid[8075] of the scandal, as no possible scandal could attach to him. This[8076] attitude of his gave me furiously to think, and I was slowly forced to[8077] the conclusion that Alfred Inglethorp wanted to be arrested. _Eh bien!_[8078] from that moment, I was equally determined that he should not be[8079] arrested.”[8080] [13]
[8081] “Wait a minute. I don’t see why he wished to be arrested?”[8082] [13]
[8083] “Because, _mon ami_, it is the law of your country that a man once[8084] acquitted can never be tried again for the same offence. Aha! but it[8085] was clever—his idea! Assuredly, he is a man of method. See here, he[8086] knew that in his position he was bound to be suspected, so he conceived[8087] the exceedingly clever idea of preparing a lot of manufactured evidence[8088] against himself. He wished to be arrested. He would then produce his[8089] irreproachable alibi—and, hey presto, he was safe for life!”[8090] [13]
[8091] “But I still don’t see how he managed to prove his alibi, and yet go to[8092] the chemist’s shop?”[8093] [13]
[8094] Poirot stared at me in surprise.[8095] [13]
[8096] “Is it possible? My poor friend! You have not yet realized that it was[8097] Miss Howard who went to the chemist’s shop?”[8098] [13]
[8099] “Miss Howard?”[8100] [13]
[8101] “But, certainly. Who else? It was most easy for her. She is of a good[8102] height, her voice is deep and manly; moreover, remember, she and[8103] Inglethorp are cousins, and there is a distinct resemblance between[8104] them, especially in their gait and bearing. It was simplicity itself.[8105] They are a clever pair!”[8106] [13]
[8107] “I am still a little fogged as to how exactly the bromide business was[8108] done,” I remarked.[8109] [13]
[8110] “_Bon!_ I will reconstruct for you as far as possible. I am inclined to[8111] think that Miss Howard was the master mind in that affair. You remember[8112] her once mentioning that her father was a doctor? Possibly she[8113] dispensed his medicines for him, or she may have taken the idea from[8114] one of the many books lying about when Mademoiselle Cynthia was[8115] studying for her exam. Anyway, she was familiar with the fact that the[8116] addition of a bromide to a mixture containing strychnine would cause[8117] the precipitation of the latter. Probably the idea came to her quite[8118] suddenly. Mrs. Inglethorp had a box of bromide powders, which she[8119] occasionally took at night. What could be easier than quietly to[8120] dissolve one or more of those powders in Mrs. Inglethorp’s large sized[8121] bottle of medicine when it came from Coot’s? The risk is practically[8122] nil. The tragedy will not take place until nearly a fortnight later. If[8123] anyone has seen either of them touching the medicine, they will have[8124] forgotten it by that time. Miss Howard will have engineered her[8125] quarrel, and departed from the house. The lapse of time, and her[8126] absence, will defeat all suspicion. Yes, it was a clever idea! If they[8127] had left it alone, it is possible the crime might never have been[8128] brought home to them. But they were not satisfied. They tried to be too[8129] clever—and that was their undoing.”[8130] [13]
[8131] Poirot puffed at his tiny cigarette, his eyes fixed on the ceiling.[8132] [13]
[8133] “They arranged a plan to throw suspicion on John Cavendish, by buying[8134] strychnine at the village chemist’s, and signing the register in his[8135] hand-writing.[8136] [13]
[8137] “On Monday Mrs. Inglethorp will take the last dose of her medicine. On[8138] Monday, therefore, at six o’clock, Alfred Inglethorp arranges to be[8139] seen by a number of people at a spot far removed from the village. Miss[8140] Howard has previously made up a cock and bull story about him and Mrs.[8141] Raikes to account for his holding his tongue afterwards. At six[8142] o’clock, Miss Howard, disguised as Alfred Inglethorp, enters the[8143] chemist’s shop, with her story about a dog, obtains the strychnine, and[8144] writes the name of Alfred Inglethorp in John’s handwriting, which she[8145] had previously studied carefully.[8146] [13]
[8147] “But, as it will never do if John, too, can prove an alibi, she writes[8148] him an anonymous note—still copying his hand-writing—which takes him to[8149] a remote spot where it is exceedingly unlikely that anyone will see[8150] him.[8151] [13]
[8152] “So far, all goes well. Miss Howard goes back to Middlingham. Alfred[8153] Inglethorp returns to Styles. There is nothing that can compromise him[8154] in any way, since it is Miss Howard who has the strychnine, which,[8155] after all, is only wanted as a blind to throw suspicion on John[8156] Cavendish.[8157] [13]
[8158] “But now a hitch occurs. Mrs. Inglethorp does not take her medicine[8159] that night. The broken bell, Cynthia’s absence—arranged by Inglethorp[8160] through his wife—all these are wasted. And then—he makes his slip.[8161] [13]
[8162] “Mrs. Inglethorp is out, and he sits down to write to his accomplice,[8163] who, he fears, may be in a panic at the non-success of their plan. It[8164] is probable that Mrs. Inglethorp returned earlier than he expected.[8165] Caught in the act, and somewhat flurried he hastily shuts and locks his[8166] desk. He fears that if he remains in the room he may have to open it[8167] again, and that Mrs. Inglethorp might catch sight of the letter before[8168] he could snatch it up. So he goes out and walks in the woods, little[8169] dreaming that Mrs. Inglethorp will open his desk, and discover the[8170] incriminating document.[8171] [13]
[8172] “But this, as we know, is what happened. Mrs. Inglethorp reads it, and[8173] becomes aware of the perfidy of her husband and Evelyn Howard, though,[8174] unfortunately, the sentence about the bromides conveys no warning to[8175] her mind. She knows that she is in danger—but is ignorant of where the[8176] danger lies. She decides to say nothing to her husband, but sits down[8177] and writes to her solicitor, asking him to come on the morrow, and she[8178] also determines to destroy immediately the will which she has just[8179] made. She keeps the fatal letter.”[8180] [13]
[8181] “It was to discover that letter, then, that her husband forced the lock[8182] of the despatch-case?”[8183] [13]
[8184] “Yes, and from the enormous risk he ran we can see how fully he[8185] realized its importance. That letter excepted, there was absolutely[8186] nothing to connect him with the crime.”[8187] [13]
[8188] “There’s only one thing I can’t make out, why didn’t he destroy it at[8189] once when he got hold of it?”[8190] [13]
[8191] “Because he did not dare take the biggest risk of all—that of keeping[8192] it on his own person.”[8193] [13]
[8194] “I don’t understand.”[8195] [13]
[8196] “Look at it from his point of view. I have discovered that there were[8197] only five short minutes in which he could have taken it—the five[8198] minutes immediately before our own arrival on the scene, for before[8199] that time Annie was brushing the stairs, and would have seen anyone who[8200] passed going to the right wing. Figure to yourself the scene! He enters[8201] the room, unlocking the door by means of one of the other doorkeys—they[8202] were all much alike. He hurries to the despatch-case—it is locked, and[8203] the keys are nowhere to be seen. That is a terrible blow to him, for it[8204] means that his presence in the room cannot be concealed as he had[8205] hoped. But he sees clearly that everything must be risked for the sake[8206] of that damning piece of evidence. Quickly, he forces the lock with a[8207] penknife, and turns over the papers until he finds what he is looking[8208] for.[8209] [13]
[8210] “But now a fresh dilemma arises: he dare not keep that piece of paper[8211] on him. He may be seen leaving the room—he may be searched. If the[8212] paper is found on him, it is certain doom. Probably, at this minute,[8213] too, he hears the sounds below of Mr. Wells and John leaving the[8214] boudoir. He must act quickly. Where can he hide this terrible slip of[8215] paper? The contents of the waste-paper-basket are kept and in any case,[8216] are sure to be examined. There are no means of destroying it; and he[8217] dare not keep it. He looks round, and he sees—what do you think, _mon[8218] ami?_”[8219] [13]
[8220] I shook my head.[8221] [13]
[8222] “In a moment, he has torn the letter into long thin strips, and rolling[8223] them up into spills he thrusts them hurriedly in amongst the other[8224] spills in the vase on the mantle-piece.”[8225] [13]
[8226] I uttered an exclamation.[8227] [13]
[8228] “No one would think of looking there,” Poirot continued. “And he will[8229] be able, at his leisure, to come back and destroy this solitary piece[8230] of evidence against him.”[8231] [13]
[8232] “Then, all the time, it was in the spill vase in Mrs. Inglethorp’s[8233] bedroom, under our very noses?” I cried.[8234] [13]
[8235] Poirot nodded.[8236] [13]
[8237] “Yes, my friend. That is where I discovered my ‘last link,’ and I owe[8238] that very fortunate discovery to you.”[8239] [13]
[8240] “To me?”[8241] [13]
[8242] “Yes. Do you remember telling me that my hand shook as I was[8243] straightening the ornaments on the mantelpiece?”[8244] [13]
[8245] “Yes, but I don’t see——”[8246] [13]
[8247] “No, but I saw. Do you know, my friend, I remembered that earlier in[8248] the morning, when we had been there together, I had straightened all[8249] the objects on the mantelpiece. And, if they were already straightened,[8250] there would be no need to straighten them again, unless, in the[8251] meantime, someone else had touched them.”[8252] [13]
[8253] “Dear me,” I murmured, “so that is the explanation of your[8254] extraordinary behaviour. You rushed down to Styles, and found it still[8255] there?”[8256] [13]
[8257] “Yes, and it was a race for time.”[8258] [13]
[8259] “But I still can’t understand why Inglethorp was such a fool as to[8260] leave it there when he had plenty of opportunity to destroy it.”[8261] [13]
[8262] “Ah, but he had no opportunity. _I_ saw to that.”[8263] [13]
[8264] “You?”[8265] [13]
[8266] “Yes. Do you remember reproving me for taking the household into my[8267] confidence on the subject?”[8268] [13]
[8269] “Yes.”[8270] [13]
[8271] “Well, my friend, I saw there was just one chance. I was not sure then[8272] if Inglethorp was the criminal or not, but if he was I reasoned that he[8273] would not have the paper on him, but would have hidden it somewhere,[8274] and by enlisting the sympathy of the household I could effectually[8275] prevent his destroying it. He was already under suspicion, and by[8276] making the matter public I secured the services of about ten amateur[8277] detectives, who would be watching him unceasingly, and being himself[8278] aware of their watchfulness he would not dare seek further to destroy[8279] the document. He was therefore forced to depart from the house, leaving[8280] it in the spill vase.”[8281] [13]
[8282] “But surely Miss Howard had ample opportunities of aiding him.”[8283] [13]
[8284] “Yes, but Miss Howard did not know of the paper’s existence. In[8285] accordance with their prearranged plan, she never spoke to Alfred[8286] Inglethorp. They were supposed to be deadly enemies, and until John[8287] Cavendish was safely convicted they neither of them dared risk a[8288] meeting. Of course I had a watch kept on Mr. Inglethorp, hoping that[8289] sooner or later he would lead me to the hiding-place. But he was too[8290] clever to take any chances. The paper was safe where it was; since no[8291] one had thought of looking there in the first week, it was not likely[8292] they would do so afterwards. But for your lucky remark, we might never[8293] have been able to bring him to justice.”[8294] [13]
[8295] “I understand that now; but when did you first begin to suspect Miss[8296] Howard?”[8297] [13]
[8298] “When I discovered that she had told a lie at the inquest about the[8299] letter she had received from Mrs. Inglethorp.”[8300] [13]
[8301] “Why, what was there to lie about?”[8302] [13]
[8303] “You saw that letter? Do you recall its general appearance?”[8304] [13]
[8305] “Yes—more or less.”[8306] [13]
[8307] “You will recollect, then, that Mrs. Inglethorp wrote a very[8308] distinctive hand, and left large clear spaces between her words. But if[8309] you look at the date at the top of the letter you will notice that[8310] ‘July 17th’ is quite different in this respect. Do you see what I[8311] mean?”[8312] [13]
[8313] “No,” I confessed, “I don’t.”[8314] [13]
[8315] “You do not see that that letter was not written on the 17th, but on[8316] the 7th—the day after Miss Howard’s departure? The ‘1’ was written in[8317] before the ‘7’ to turn it into the ‘17th’.”[8318] [13]
[8319] “But why?”[8320] [13]
[8321] “That is exactly what I asked myself. Why does Miss Howard suppress the[8322] letter written on the 17th, and produce this faked one instead? Because[8323] she did not wish to show the letter of the 17th. Why, again? And at[8324] once a suspicion dawned in my mind. You will remember my saying that it[8325] was wise to beware of people who were not telling you the truth.”[8326] [13]
[8327] “And yet,” I cried indignantly, “after that, you gave me two reasons[8328] why Miss Howard could not have committed the crime!”[8329] [13]
[8330] “And very good reasons too,” replied Poirot. “For a long time they were[8331] a stumbling-block to me until I remembered a very significant fact:[8332] that she and Alfred Inglethorp were cousins. She could not have[8333] committed the crime single-handed, but the reasons against that did not[8334] debar her from being an accomplice. And, then, there was that rather[8335] over-vehement hatred of hers! It concealed a very opposite emotion.[8336] There was, undoubtedly, a tie of passion between them long before he[8337] came to Styles. They had already arranged their infamous plot—that he[8338] should marry this rich, but rather foolish old lady, induce her to make[8339] a will leaving her money to him, and then gain their ends by a very[8340] cleverly conceived crime. If all had gone as they planned, they would[8341] probably have left England, and lived together on their poor victim’s[8342] money.[8343] [13]
[8344] “They are a very astute and unscrupulous pair. While suspicion was to[8345] be directed against him, she would be making quiet preparations for a[8346] very different _dénouement_. She arrives from Middlingham with all the[8347] compromising items in her possession. No suspicion attaches to her. No[8348] notice is paid to her coming and going in the house. She hides the[8349] strychnine and glasses in John’s room. She puts the beard in the attic.[8350] She will see to it that sooner or later they are duly discovered.”[8351] [13]
[8352] “I don’t quite see why they tried to fix the blame on John,” I[8353] remarked. “It would have been much easier for them to bring the crime[8354] home to Lawrence.”[8355] [13]
[8356] “Yes, but that was mere chance. All the evidence against him arose out[8357] of pure accident. It must, in fact, have been distinctly annoying to[8358] the pair of schemers.”[8359] [13]
[8360] “His manner was unfortunate,” I observed thoughtfully.[8361] [13]
[8362] “Yes. You realize, of course, what was at the back of that?”[8363] [13]
[8364] “No.”[8365] [13]
[8366] “You did not understand that he believed Mademoiselle Cynthia guilty of[8367] the crime?”[8368] [13]
[8369] “No,” I exclaimed, astonished. “Impossible!”[8370] [13]
[8371] “Not at all. I myself nearly had the same idea. It was in my mind when[8372] I asked Mr. Wells that first question about the will. Then there were[8373] the bromide powders which she had made up, and her clever male[8374] impersonations, as Dorcas recounted them to us. There was really more[8375] evidence against her than anyone else.”[8376] [13]
[8377] “You are joking, Poirot!”[8378] [13]
[8379] “No. Shall I tell you what made Monsieur Lawrence turn so pale when he[8380] first entered his mother’s room on the fatal night? It was because,[8381] whilst his mother lay there, obviously poisoned, he saw, over your[8382] shoulder, that the door into Mademoiselle Cynthia’s room was unbolted.”[8383] [13]
[8384] “But he declared that he saw it bolted!” I cried.[8385] [13]
[8386] “Exactly,” said Poirot dryly. “And that was just what confirmed my[8387] suspicion that it was not. He was shielding Mademoiselle Cynthia.”[8388] [13]
[8389] “But why should he shield her?”[8390] [13]
[8391] “Because he is in love with her.”[8392] [13]
[8393] I laughed.[8394] [13]
[8395] “There, Poirot, you are quite wrong! I happen to know for a fact that,[8396] far from being in love with her, he positively dislikes her.”[8397] [13]
[8398] “Who told you that, _mon ami?_”[8399] [13]
[8400] “Cynthia herself.”[8401] [13]
[8402] “_La pauvre petite!_ And she was concerned?”[8403] [13]
[8404] “She said that she did not mind at all.”[8405] [13]
[8406] “Then she certainly did mind very much,” remarked Poirot. “They are[8407] like that—_les femmes!_”[8408] [13]
[8409] “What you say about Lawrence is a great surprise to me,” I said.[8410] [13]
[8411] “But why? It was most obvious. Did not Monsieur Lawrence make the sour[8412] face every time Mademoiselle Cynthia spoke and laughed with his[8413] brother? He had taken it into his long head that Mademoiselle Cynthia[8414] was in love with Monsieur John. When he entered his mother’s room, and[8415] saw her obviously poisoned, he jumped to the conclusion that[8416] Mademoiselle Cynthia knew something about the matter. He was nearly[8417] driven desperate. First he crushed the coffee-cup to powder under his[8418] feet, remembering that _she_ had gone up with his mother the night[8419] before, and he determined that there should be no chance of testing its[8420] contents. Thenceforward, he strenuously, and quite uselessly, upheld[8421] the theory of ‘Death from natural causes’.”[8422] [13]
[8423] “And what about the ‘extra coffee-cup’?”[8424] [13]
[8425] “I was fairly certain that it was Mrs. Cavendish who had hidden it, but[8426] I had to make sure. Monsieur Lawrence did not know at all what I meant;[8427] but, on reflection, he came to the conclusion that if he could find an[8428] extra coffee-cup anywhere his lady love would be cleared of suspicion.[8429] And he was perfectly right.”[8430] [13]
[8431] “One thing more. What did Mrs. Inglethorp mean by her dying words?”[8432] [13]
[8433] “They were, of course, an accusation against her husband.”[8434] [13]
[8435] “Dear me, Poirot,” I said with a sigh, “I think you have explained[8436] everything. I am glad it has all ended so happily. Even John and his[8437] wife are reconciled.”[8438] [13]
[8439] “Thanks to me.”[8440] [13]
[8441] “How do you mean—thanks to you?”[8442] [13]
[8443] “My dear friend, do you not realize that it was simply and solely the[8444] trial which has brought them together again? That John Cavendish still[8445] loved his wife, I was convinced. Also, that she was equally in love[8446] with him. But they had drifted very far apart. It all arose from a[8447] misunderstanding. She married him without love. He knew it. He is a[8448] sensitive man in his way, he would not force himself upon her if she[8449] did not want him. And, as he withdrew, her love awoke. But they are[8450] both unusually proud, and their pride held them inexorably apart. He[8451] drifted into an entanglement with Mrs. Raikes, and she deliberately[8452] cultivated the friendship of Dr. Bauerstein. Do you remember the day of[8453] John Cavendish’s arrest, when you found me deliberating over a big[8454] decision?”[8455] [13]
[8456] “Yes, I quite understood your distress.”[8457] [13]
[8458] “Pardon me, _mon ami_, but you did not understand it in the least. I[8459] was trying to decide whether or not I would clear John Cavendish at[8460] once. I could have cleared him—though it might have meant a failure to[8461] convict the real criminals. They were entirely in the dark as to my[8462] real attitude up to the very last moment—which partly accounts for my[8463] success.”[8464] [13]
[8465] “Do you mean that you could have saved John Cavendish from being[8466] brought to trial?”[8467] [13]
[8468] “Yes, my friend. But I eventually decided in favour of ‘a woman’s[8469] happiness’. Nothing but the great danger through which they have passed[8470] could have brought these two proud souls together again.”[8471] [13]
[8472] I looked at Poirot in silent amazement. The colossal cheek of the[8473] little man! Who on earth but Poirot would have thought of a trial for[8474] murder as a restorer of conjugal happiness![8475] [13]
[8476] “I perceive your thoughts, _mon ami_,” said Poirot, smiling at me. “No[8477] one but Hercule Poirot would have attempted such a thing! And you are[8478] wrong in condemning it. The happiness of one man and one woman is the[8479] greatest thing in all the world.”[8480] [13]
[8481] His words took me back to earlier events. I remembered Mary as she lay[8482] white and exhausted on the sofa, listening, listening. There had come[8483] the sound of the bell below. She had started up. Poirot had opened the[8484] door, and meeting her agonized eyes had nodded gently. “Yes, madame,”[8485] he said. “I have brought him back to you.” He had stood aside, and as I[8486] went out I had seen the look in Mary’s eyes, as John Cavendish had[8487] caught his wife in his arms.[8488] [13]
[8489] “Perhaps you are right, Poirot,” I said gently. “Yes, it is the[8490] greatest thing in the world.”[8491] [13]
[8492] Suddenly, there was a tap at the door, and Cynthia peeped in.[8493] [13]
[8494] “I—I only——”[8495] [13]
[8496] “Come in,” I said, springing up.[8497] [13]
[8498] She came in, but did not sit down.[8499] [13]
[8500] “I—only wanted to tell you something——”[8501] [13]
[8502] “Yes?”[8503] [13]
[8504] Cynthia fidgeted with a little tassel for some moments, then, suddenly[8505] exclaiming: “You dears!” kissed first me and then Poirot, and rushed[8506] out of the room again.[8507] [13]
[8508] “What on earth does this mean?” I asked, surprised.[8509] [13]
[8510] It was very nice to be kissed by Cynthia, but the publicity of the[8511] salute rather impaired the pleasure.[8512] [13]
[8513] “It means that she has discovered Monsieur Lawrence does not dislike[8514] her as much as she thought,” replied Poirot philosophically.[8515] [13]
[8516] “But——”[8517] [13]
[8518] “Here he is.”[8519] [13]
[8520] Lawrence at that moment passed the door.[8521] [13]
[8522] “Eh! Monsieur Lawrence,” called Poirot. “We must congratulate you, is[8523] it not so?”[8524] [13]
[8525] Lawrence blushed, and then smiled awkwardly. A man in love is a sorry[8526] spectacle. Now Cynthia had looked charming.[8527] [13]
[8528] I sighed.[8529] [13]
[8530] “What is it, _mon ami?_”[8531] [13]
[8532] “Nothing,” I said sadly. “They are two delightful women!”[8533] [13]
[8534] “And neither of them is for you?” finished Poirot. “Never mind. Console[8535] yourself, my friend. We may hunt together again, who knows? And then——”[8536] [13]
[8537] [13]
[8538] THE END[8539] [13]
[8540] [13]
[8541] [13]
